The Proof of Temporal Origination Does Not Require Attributes to Be Actual Existents
Sanusi concedes the intermediate state and argues the proof still holds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of the world's temporal origination remains valid even if the attributes of bodies are not actual existents, but merely established.

Core Idea
Establishment of attributes suffices for the proof of temporal origination.

Sanusi now addresses a sophisticated objection from opponents who deny the actual existence of attributes but concede their establishment (thubūt). He shows that even on their own terms, the proof of temporal origination holds. This passage demonstrates a key logical principle in kalām: what matters for a proof is not the ontological status of the properties but their necessary connection to the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concessive tone in the opening: "We grant the existence of the intermediate state."
2
Identify the logical steps
Trace the reasoning: (1) attributes are established, (2) they necessitate temporal origination, (3) so bodies must be temporal. Even if attributes are not existent, their establishment is enough.
The key is the distinction between existence (wujūd) and establishment (thubūt).
3
Understand the rebuttal
Sanusi argues that objecting to non-existence while granting establishment is harmless. The only way to harm the proof is to deny establishment entirely, which is self-evidently false.
4
Reflect on the theologians' debate
Note Sanusi's observation that earlier theologians argued unnecessarily about the existence of attributes. The proof does not require that level of detail.
This shows practical wisdom in kalām: avoid unnecessary extensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلَّمنا ثبوت الواسطة ، فيلزمُ أنَّ الأجرام تلازم صفاتٍ ثابتةً وجبَ لها الحدوث ، فيلزم حدوثها ضرورةً ، فقد تم البرهان على حدوثِ العالم على أكمل وجهِ بمُجرَّدِ ثبوتِ هذهِ الصفاتِ ، وإن لم تنته إلى درجة الوجودِ ، فالقدح بعدم وجودِها مع تسليم ثبوتها لا يضرُّ شيئاً في دليل الحدوثِ ، وإِنَّما يضرُّ بالدليل الإصرار على عدمها ، وهو باطل على الضرورة ، وقد أطال المتكلمون معهم في الاستدلال على وجودها مِنْ غيرِ حاجةٍ إليهِ أصلاً
We grant the existence of the intermediate state (wāsiṭa). Then it follows that bodies are necessarily connected to established attributes which necessitate their temporal origination (ḥudūth), so their temporal origination necessarily follows. Thus the proof of the temporal origination of the world is completed in the most perfect manner merely by the establishment of these attributes, even if they do not reach the degree of existence. Thus objecting to their non-existence while granting their establishment does not harm the proof of temporal origination at all. Rather, the proof is harmed only by insisting on their non-existence, which is evidently false. The theologians (mutakallimūn) have argued with them at length about their existence without any need for it at all.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection regarding the intermediate state (al-wāsiṭa) or the theory of states (aḥwāl). The author adopts a concessive approach: even if we grant the opponent's premise that the attributes (ṣifāt) of bodies are not actual existents (mawjūdāt) but merely established (thābita), the proof of the world's temporal origination still holds. The author explains that bodies necessarily have attributes that necessitate their temporality, and once those attributes are established, the temporal origination of bodies follows necessarily. The proof is complete regardless of whether these attributes reach the level of existence. Therefore, objecting to their non-existence while conceding their establishment is irrelevant. The only way to undermine the proof is to deny the establishment of these attributes outright, which is self-evidently false. The author notes that theologians unnecessarily prolonged debates about the existence of these attributes.

الصفات
الحدوث
ثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of temporal origination requires attributes to be actual existents.
Correction: Sanusi shows that establishment (thubūt) is sufficient; existence is not required.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is sufficient for the proof of the temporal origination of the world?
A) The actual existence of the attributes
B) The establishment of the attributes
C) The insistence on the non-existence of attributes
D) The lengthy arguments of theologians

Key Takeaway
The proof of the world's temporal origination does not depend on the actual existence of the attributes of bodies; their establishment is sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a truth about your relationship with Allah that you recognize as 'established' in your mind (like the attributes of bodies here) but you haven't acted upon because you feel it isn't 'real' enough? What would change if you treated that established truth as fully sufficient for action—starting today?
?
Sanusi points out that insisting on non-existence harms the proof. In your own life, are you holding out for a level of certainty about some Islamic teaching that is higher than what is required, thereby delaying obedience? Identify one teaching where you can accept its 'establishment' as sufficient and commit to living by it this week.
?
The theologians argued at length about the existence of attributes unnecessarily. Reflect: Are you wasting mental energy debating issues that do not impact your faith or practice, while the simpler, established proofs are already enough? What is one debate you need to set aside to focus on what is already clear?

Proof That Accidents Cannot Subsist by Themselves
Sanusi proves that motion cannot subsist in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents like motion cannot exist independently and must inhere in a substance, through Sanusi's two-part proof.

Core Idea
An accident's very definition proves it cannot stand alone.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a concise yet powerful proof that accidents cannot subsist by themselves. He uses the familiar example of motion to illustrate both a definitional contradiction and an argument from infinite regress. By examining motion's reality, he shows that requiring it to be self-subsistent leads to absurdities, confirming that all accidents need a substance to inhere in.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without translation, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the definition, then two conditional arguments.
2
Understand the definition of motion
The author defines motion as the transfer of a substance. This definition is key to the proof. Ask yourself: if motion is defined as a transfer, can it transfer itself?
3
Follow the first argument: definitional contradiction
If motion subsisted by itself, it would have to transfer itself, but that contradicts its definition as a transfer of another substance. So self-subsistence is impossible.
4
Follow the second argument: infinite regress
If motion transferred itself, that act of transferring would itself require another transfer, leading to an infinite chain. This is impossible, so motion cannot self-subsist.
5
Conclude: accidents need substances
Therefore, motion and all accidents must inhere in a substance. This establishes a foundational principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركة مثلاً حقيقتها انتقال جوهرٍ مِنْ حيّز إلى حيز، فلو قامَتْ بنفسها أو انتقلَتْ هي لزم قلبُ هذه الحقيقة. وأيضاً: لو انتقلَتْ لزمَ قيامُ انتقال بها، وذلك الانتقال ينتقل أيضاً، فيقومُ بهِ انتقال؛ وذلك يؤدي إلى التسلسل، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى.
For example, motion's reality is the transfer of a substance from one location to another. So if it (motion) subsisted by itself or transferred itself, then the reversal of this reality would be necessary. Also: if it transferred, then the existence of a transfer would be necessary in it, and that transfer would also transfer, so a transfer would subsist in it; and that leads to infinite regress and the subsistence of an accident in an accident.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that accidents cannot subsist in themselves. The author defines motion as an accident that inheres in a substance, involving the transfer of a substance. If motion were to subsist by itself (i.e., be a substance), then it would have to transfer itself, which contradicts its definition as a transfer of another substance. Furthermore, if motion transferred itself, that act of transferring would itself require another transfer ad infinitum, leading to an infinite regress and the impossibility of an accident (like motion) subsisting in another accident. The argument establishes that all accidents must inhere in a substance.

Key Terms
حركة
جوهر
انتقال
قيام
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion can exist independently without a substance.
Correction: Sanusi proves that motion's definition as a transfer of a substance means it cannot subsist by itself; doing so either contradicts its definition or leads to infinite regress, so it must inhere in a substance.

Checkpoint
What does the author use the example of motion to prove?
A) Motion is a substance.
B) Accidents cannot subsist in themselves.
C) Motion can transfer itself.
D) Infinite regress is possible.

Key Takeaway
Motion and all accidents must inhere in a substance; they cannot subsist by themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the motion of your body depends entirely on a substance that Allah creates moment by moment, then what part of your life can you truly claim as your own independent achievement? How does this reshape your gratitude for every single action you perform today?
?
The proof shows that accidents cannot stand alone—they need a substance. In your own life, what 'accidents' of character, habit, or belief have you been treating as if they could subsist on their own? Will you examine them today and anchor them in the substance of truth?
?
You know that infinite regress is impossible. Yet how often do you let your mind run in endless loops of worry or desire without stopping at the First Cause? When will you cut the regress and submit to Allah, the only Self-Subsistent?

Philosophers' Objection to the Major Premise
Sanusi presents the philosophers' objection to the major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection to the major premise in the proof of the world's temporal origin and why it challenges the argument.

Core Idea
The philosophers deny that temporally originated attributes prove a temporally originated subject.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a crucial objection raised by philosophers against one of the key premises in the proof of the world's temporal origin. The objection targets the major premise that if an entity's attributes are temporally originated, then the entity itself must be temporally originated. This challenges the very structure of the argument. Let's examine the objection and understand why it demands a response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the objection.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the major premise
Find the statement that the philosophers object to: 'Whoever's attributes are temporally originated, then he is temporally originated.'
3
Understand the objection
The philosophers say this premise is not conceded. They believe an eternal being can have temporally originated attributes, so the premise is false.
This objection requires a response, which follows later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت هذا فقولنا: وتقديرها حوادث لا مبدأ لها أي: تقدير صفاتِ العالم.. اعتراضُ مِنَ الفلاسفة على كبرى الدليل الذي استدللنا به على حدوث العالم؛ وهي قولنا: وكلُّ مَنْ صفاتُه حادثةٌ فهو حادث. ووجه الاعتراض: أنَّهم قالوا: لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّ مَنْ صفاتُه حادثةٌ فهو حادث.
When you have understood this, our statement: 'And considering them as accidents with no beginning' means: considering the attributes of the world... is an objection from the philosophers against the major premise of the proof we used to prove the temporal origin of the world, which is our statement: 'And whoever's attributes are temporally originated, then he is temporally originated.' And the aspect of the objection is that they said: 'It is not conceded that whoever's attributes are temporally originated, then he is temporally originated.'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection (i'tirad) to the key premise in the proof of the world's temporal origin. The author explains that the phrase 'considering them as accidents with no beginning' refers to an objection targeting the major premise (kubra) of the proof. The premise is: 'Whoever's attributes are temporally originated, then he (the subject) is temporally originated.' The philosophers object by denying this premise—they do not concede that temporally originated attributes necessitate a temporally originated subject, since they believe in an eternal subject with temporally originated attributes (like motions). The author notes the objection but does not provide the answer in this passage; the answer presumably follows in the subsequent text.

Key Terms

كبرى
دليل
صفات
حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise that temporally originated attributes imply a temporally originated subject is obviously true.
Correction: The philosophers reject this premise, arguing that an eternal being can have temporally originated attributes, so the premise is not universally true.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' objection to the proof of the world's temporality?
A) They deny that the world has attributes.
B) They reject the major premise that temporally originated attributes imply a temporally originated subject.
C) They claim that the world is eternal in all respects.
D) They affirm the existence of accidents.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers object to the proof of the world's temporal origin by denying that temporally originated attributes necessitate a temporally originated subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who denies a seemingly obvious truth—like the principle that temporal attributes imply a temporal subject—what is your first reaction? Do you feel defensive, curious, or dismissive? How can this lesson help you prepare a reasoned response?
?
The philosophers' objection challenges a foundational premise of your belief in the world's creation. Are there foundational premises in your own faith that you hold without truly examining them? What would it take for you to investigate them deeply?
?
If you were faced with a skeptic who rejects your most basic assumptions, would you be able to articulate the proof step by step, as the theologians do? What would you need to study to be ready for that conversation? Commit to one specific action to strengthen your understanding.

Refuting the Philosophers' Objection on Infinite Events
Sanusi presents the philosophers' objection and gives the first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that events have no beginning and the first kalam refutation showing that a completed infinite is contradictory.

Core Idea
A completed infinite series is logically impossible.

The philosophers argued that events could be infinite in the past, thus avoiding the need for a first event. Sanusi presents this objection and then delivers a powerful refutation: if there were an infinite number of past events, they would have to be completed by now, but an infinite cannot be completed. This contradiction shows the impossibility of an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Carefully read the objection where they claim events have no beginning. Notice how they grant the minor premise but deny the conclusion.
The objection targets the major premise of the proof.
2
Identify the key claim
The philosophers assert that for any event, there is always a prior event ad infinitum. This means the series of past events has no first member.
3
Study the first answer
Sanusi argues that if past events have no beginning, then an infinite number must have already occurred. But completion and infinity are contradictory. Reflect on why this is a necessary impossibility.
This is a logical contradiction, not a physical one.
4
Consider the corollary
If the completion of an infinite series is impossible, then our present existence, which depends on past events, would also be impossible. This strengthens the refutation.
This shows the absurd consequence of the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت هذا فقولنا : ) وتقديرها حوادث لا مبدأ لها) أي : تقدير صفاتِ العالم .. اعتراضُ مِنَ الفلاسفة على كبرى الدليل الذي استدللنا به على حدوث العالم ؛ وهي قولنا : ( وكلُّ مَنْ صفاته حادثةٌ فهو حادث )
ووجه الاعتراض : أنَّهم قالوا : لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّ مَنْ صفاتُه حادثةٌ فهو حادث ، قولكم : ( لأنَّه لا يعرى عنها ) مُسلَّمٌ ، وقولكم : ( فيكونُ حادثاً مثلها ( ممنوع ؛ لأنَّ ذلك إنَّما يلزم لو كانَتِ الحوادث التي لازمَتِ الأجرام لها مبدأ يُفتتَحُ بهِ عددُها ، ونحن نقولُ : لا مُفتَحَ لتلك الحوادثِ ، بل ما مِنْ حادث إلا وقبلَهُ حادث لا إلى أوَّل ، فلم يلزم مِنْ قدمِ الأجرام على هذا التقديرِ عُرُؤُها عنِ الحوادثِ اللازمة لها ؛ لأنَّ نوعها الذي لا تنفكُ عنه الأجرام قديم
والجواب مِنْ أوجه :
(۱)
الأوّلُ : أَنَّهُ يلزم على وجودِ حوادث لا أول لها : أن يكون دخل في الوجودِ وفرغَ مِنْ حركاتِ الأفلاكِ وأشخاص الحيوان ونحوها على الترتيب واحداً بعد واحد.. عدد لا نهاية له ! والجمعُ بينَ الفراغِ وعدم النهايةِ جمع بين متناقضين ، فيكون محالاً على الضرورة ،
ويلزمُ عليهِ : أنْ يكونَ وجودنا ووجود سائر الحوادثِ الآنَ محالاً لتوقفه على المحالِ ، وهو فراغ ما لا نهاية له ، وإلى هذا الجواب أشرنا في العقيدة بقولنا : ( يؤدّي . . . ( إلى آخرِهِ ، و(مِنْ) في قولنا : ( مِنْ وجودِ الحوادثِ ( لبيان ( ما ) الموصولة قبلها ، والضمير في ( عليه ( يعودُ على ( فراغ ما لا نهاية له ) ؛ أي : فما توقف على فراغ ما لا نهاية له الذي اتضحَتِ استحالته . . يجب أن يكون محالاً ؛
(۱) وعليه : فأشخاص العالم حادثة عندهم، وأنواعها قديمة، فهو قول بالقدم النوعي لأشخاصها فقط، مع التسليم بحدوث أفرادها، وسيبين المصنف المحالات العقلية اللازمة على إثبات هذا
So if you know this, then our statement: 'And their estimation that events have no beginning' i.e., the estimation of the attributes of the world... is an objection of the philosophers against the major premise of the proof we used for the temporality of the world; and it is our statement: 'And every being whose attributes are contingent is itself contingent.'
And the face of the objection is that they said: It is not granted that whoever has contingent attributes is contingent. Your statement 'because it is not free of them' is granted, and your statement 'so it becomes contingent like them' is denied; because that follows only if the events that are inseparable from bodies have a beginning by which their number is opened. And we say: there is no beginning for those events; rather, no event occurs except that before it is an event without a beginning. So it does not follow from the eternity of bodies on this estimation that they are free of the events necessary for them; because their kind, from which bodies cannot be free, is eternal.
And the answer is from aspects:
(1) The first: that upon the existence of events with no first one, it would follow that there has entered into existence and been completed of the movements of the spheres and individuals of animals and the like in order one after another... a number with no end! And combining completion and infinity is combining two contradictories, so it is impossible by necessity.
And it follows from that that our existence and the existence of all other events now would be impossible because they depend on the impossible, which is the completion of what has no end. And to this answer we alluded in the Creed by our saying 'it leads...' to its end. And 'min' in our statement 'min wujud al-hawadith' is to explain 'ma' the relative pronoun before it, and the pronoun in 'alayhi' refers to 'faragh ma la nihaya lahu' i.e., whatever depends on the completion of what has no end, which has become evident as impossible... must be impossible.
(1) Accordingly: so the individuals of the world are contingent according to them, and their kinds eternal; so it is a statement of the specific eternity of their individuals only, while conceding the temporality of their particulars; and the author will explain the rational impossibilities entailed by affirming this.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection to the kalam proof for the world's temporality and the first answer. The proof's major premise is 'every being with contingent attributes is itself contingent.' The philosophers object: we grant that the world is never free of events, but we deny that it becomes contingent like them. This would only follow if the events had a first beginning; but we say events have no first—before each event is another ad infinitum. Thus the world can be eternal despite having forever-contingent attributes. 

The author answers from multiple aspects; here is the first. If events have no first, then an infinite number of events (e.g., celestial movements, animal individuals) must have already been completed in the past. But 'completion' and 'infinite' are contradictory: a series cannot be both completed and infinite. Therefore, such an infinite regress is impossible by logical necessity. As a corollary, the present existence of ourselves and all current events would be impossible because they depend on the impossible completion of an infinite series. The author notes that this answer was alluded to in his Creed. The footnote clarifies that the philosophers' position is that individuals are contingent but their kind is eternal, which the author will show leads to rational absurdities.

Key Terms
كبرى الدليل
حوادث لا مبدأ لها
فراغ ما لا نهاية له
القدم النوعي
متناقضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The past could be infinite with no beginning; infinite regress is possible.
Correction: An infinite series cannot be completed; if there were no first event, then an infinite number of events would have to have already occurred, which is contradictory.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction that the first answer identifies in the philosophers' position?
A) The world is eternal yet has beginning.
B) An infinite number of events cannot be completed.
C) Events are both eternal and contingent.
D) The world cannot have attributes.

Key Takeaway
The first refutation shows that a past infinite regress of events leads to a logical contradiction, thus the world must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your every action is part of an infinite chain of causes with no first cause, would you have any real responsibility or accountability? How does the impossibility of an infinite past affect your personal sense of moral responsibility before a Creator?
?
The argument shows that your own existence depends on a finite number of past events. Does that make you feel more dependent on God? How can you live today with the awareness that your existence is not accidental but contingent on a First Cause?
?
The philosophers' objection suggests that the world could be eternal without a beginning. If that were true, what would be the basis for hope in an afterlife or judgment? How does the logical necessity of a beginning for the world give you confidence in a purposeful creation?

Second Proof: Infinite Events Lead to Contradiction
Sanusi presents the second proof showing infinite events necessitate existence with nonexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's second proof demonstrates that an infinite past of events entails the impossible conjunction of existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
An infinite past forces existence and non-existence to coexist—an impossibility.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a second, more intricate proof against the possibility of an infinite regress of temporal events. The argument shows that if events had no beginning, then the very same event would both exist and not exist eternally, which is rationally impossible. This proof strengthens the case for a temporal beginning of the universe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the thesis and conclusion
The thesis is that events with no beginning are impossible. The conclusion is that such a scenario leads to a contradiction.
3
Follow the entailment chain
Trace each step: every event is preceded by beginningless non-existence; all non-existences are simultaneous in pre-eternity; the genus of events is eternal; the genus is realized in a particular event; that event would be eternal but its non-existence is also eternal → co-occurrence of existence and non-existence.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Consider the objection and refutation
The opponent might claim non-existence precedes all events. Sanusi shows this implies a first event, contradicting no beginning.
This is a classic reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : يلزم على وجودِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها أنْ يقارنَ الوجودَ الأزليَّ عدمُهُ هذا وجة ثانٍ لإبطالِ حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وتقريره : أن نقول : لو كانت الحوادث لا أوَّلَ لها لزم اجتماع الوجودِ الأزلي مع عدمِهِ . وبيانُ الملازمةِ : أَنَّ كلَّ حادثٍ مِنْ تلك الحوادث مسبوق بعدم لا أوَّلَ لهُ ، وتلك العدماتُ كلُّها مجتمعة في الأزلِ ؛ إذ لا ترتيب فيها ، وجنسُ الحوادثِ أزليّ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّها لا أَوَّلَ لها ، وذلكَ الجنسُ لا يتحقق وجودُهُ إلا في حادثٍ مِنْ أفرادِهِ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الحادث أزليّاً ، لكن عدمُهُ السابق عليه أيضاً أزلي ؛ لما سبق أنَّ عدم كل حادث أزلي ، فقد لزمَ مقارنةً وجودِ الشيء لعدمِهِ ، لأنَّهما أزليَّانِ معاً ، واجتماعُ وجودِ الشيء معَ عدمِهِ محال على الضرورة ، وفيه أيضاً مصاحبةُ السابق - وهو العدمُ - للمسبوق ؛ وهو الوجود الحادث ، وفيهِ الجمعُ بينَ متناقضينِ ؛ وهو الحدوث والأزليَّة . فإن قالوا : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ العدمَ يصاحبه شيءٌ مِنَ الحوادثِ ، بل العدمُ قبلَ جميعها .. لزمَ أنْ يكونَ لجميع الحوادثِ أول ، وهم يقولون : لا أوَّلَ لها ، هذا خُلْفٌ وتهافت في القول ؛ وجود سابق ومسبوق في الأزلِ ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ
Also: It is necessary, given the existence of events with no beginning, that eternal existence be accompanied by its non-existence. This is a second way to invalidate events with no beginning. Its exposition: we say: If events had no beginning, then the conjunction of eternal existence with its non-existence would be necessary. The explanation of the entailment: Every event from among those events is preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning, and all those non-existences are combined in pre-eternity (al-azal), because there is no order among them. The genus (jins) of events is also eternal because they have no beginning, and that genus is actualized in terms of its existence only in some particular event from among its individuals. So it would be necessary for that event to be eternal. But its prior non-existence is also eternal, because it was established that the non-existence of every event is eternal. Thus the co-occurrence of existence and non-existence of a thing has become necessary, because both are eternal together. And the conjunction of a thing's existence with its non-existence is impossible by necessity. Also in this is the accompaniment of the prior (which is non-existence) with the posterior (which is the temporal existence). And in this is the combination of two contradictories: temporality (huduth) and eternity (azaliyya). If they say: We do not accept that non-existence is accompanied by any event; rather, non-existence is before all of them... then it would necessarily follow that all events have a beginning, while they claim they have no beginning. This is a contradiction and inconsistency in the statement: the existence of a prior and a posterior in pre-eternity, and that is not intelligible.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction (burhan khulfi) against the possibility of an infinite regress of temporal events (hawadith la awwala laha). The author, Sanusi, presents it as the 'second way'. He begins by stating the thesis: if events had no beginning, then eternal existence (al-wujud al-azali) would have to coexist with its own non-existence (adam). He then explains the entailment (bayyan al-mulazama): Every individual event is preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning; all these non-existences are simultaneous in pre-eternity (al-azal) because they have no temporal order. The genus of events (jins al-hawadith) is also eternal, since it has no beginning. But the genus is actualized only in a particular individual event. Therefore, that individual event would have to be eternal (azali). However, that same individual event is preceded by its own non-existence, which is also eternal. Hence, the existence and non-existence of the same thing would both be eternal, necessitating their co-occurrence, which is impossible by necessity (muhal 'ala al-darura). Additionally, this would entail the prior (non-existence) accompanying the posterior (temporal existence), and the combination of the contradictories of temporality (huduth) and eternity (azaliyya). The author then anticipates an objection: the opponent might say that non-existence is not accompanied by any event; rather, non-existence is before all events. The refutation: if non-existence is before all events, then the events would have a first event, contradicting their claim of no beginning. Thus the objection collapses into self-contradiction (khulf wa tahaful). This makes the original thesis impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof claims the genus itself is eternal, but the author specifies the genus is eternal because it has no beginning, and then argues that the individual event in which the genus is actualized would thereby be eternal, leading to contradiction.
Correction: The genus is eternal only in the sense of having no beginning, but the particular event is what is at issue: if the genus is actualized in a particular event, that event would have to be eternal, yet its non-existence is also eternal, creating an impossible co-occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's second proof, what is the impossible consequence of assuming events with no beginning?
A) Events would cease to occur.
B) Existence and non-existence of the same thing would be co-eternal.
C) The genus of events would not exist.
D) The events would have a beginning after all.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's second proof shows that an infinite past creates an impossible contradiction between existence and non-existence, proving that the universe must have had a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe had no beginning, your life would be lost in an endless chain of events with no ultimate purpose. Now that you know it does have a beginning, how does that change the weight you give to every action you take today?
?
The proof shows that an infinite past is logically impossible. Are you living as if your time is infinite, or are you treating each moment as the finite gift it is? What would you do differently if you truly believed your time is limited?
?
Sanusi forces his opponent to admit that denying a beginning leads to self-contradiction. Where in your own beliefs or actions are you holding onto a contradiction—something that logic tells you is false but you still act as if true? What will you do about it this week?

The Proof of Truncation and Application
Sanusi presents the proof of truncation and application against an infinite past.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof of truncation and application and why it refutes an infinite past.

Core Idea
Two infinite series can neither be equal nor unequal—a contradiction that proves a beginning.

Sanusi now presents a third and more technical argument against the possibility of an infinite past. This proof uses a simple comparison of two series of events to show that an infinite past leads to an absurdity. We must think carefully about what happens when we compare two infinite collections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the name of the proof and the logical structure: a conditional argument.
The proof is called burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq.
2
Identify the conditional
Find the 'if...then' structure: if events without beginning existed, then there would be two numbers that are neither equal nor one greater. This is the core.
The consequent is impossible by the law of excluded middle between two numbers.
3
Understand the entailment explanation
Sanusi explains why such two numbers arise: consider the series from a fixed past event (like the Flood) to pre-eternity, and from now to pre-eternity. They are different due to increase, but both are infinite so neither is exhausted.
This shows why equality and inequality are both impossible.
4
Reflect on the absurdity
The proof forces a logical contradiction, therefore the assumption (infinite past) must be false. Reflect on how this argument differs from the first two proofs.
This proof does not rely on infinite regress but on comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنْ يستحيل عندَ تطبيق ما فرغ منها بدون زيادة على نفسِهِ معَ زيادةٍ .. ما عُلِمَ بينَ العددينِ مِنْ وجوبِ المساواة أو نقيضها هذا طريق ثالث لإبطالِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها ، ويُسمَّى هذا البرهان برهان القطع والتطبيق ، وتقريره : أن نقول : لو وُجِدَتْ حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها لَلَزِمَ أنْ يُوجَدَ عددانِ متغايرانِ ، وليس أحدهما أكثر مِنَ الآخر ولا مساوياً لهُ ! والتالي باطل على الضرورةِ ؛ لما عُلِمَ مِنْ وجوب إحدى النسبتين بين كل عددين ، فيكون ملزومه ـ وهو وجود حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ـ باطلاً . وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّا لو نظرنا عدد الحوادثِ مِنَ الطوفانِ مثلاً إلى الأزلِ معَ عددِها مِنَ الآنَ مثلاً إلى الأزلِ . . لكانا عددين متغايرين على الضرورة ، وتستحيل بينهما المساواة ؛ لتحققِ الزيادة في أحدهما والشيءُ دونَ زيادةٍ لا يكون مساوياً لنفسِهِ بعد زيادةٍ ، ويستحيلُ أيضاً أنْ يكون أحدهما أكثرَ مِنَ الآخرِ ؛ لعدم تناهي أفراد كل واحدٍ منهما ، فلا يفرغ أحدهما بالعد قبل الآخرِ ، وحقيقة الأقل : ما يصيرُ عندَ العد فانياً
And that it is impossible, when applying what has been completed without increase to itself with increase... what is known between two numbers of the necessity of equality or its opposite. This is a third way to invalidate events with no beginning, and this proof is called the proof of truncation and application (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq). Its exposition: we say: If events existed with no beginning, it would necessitate that there exist two different numbers, neither of which is greater than the other nor equal to it! The consequent is necessarily false because it is known that one of the two relations is necessary between any two numbers. Therefore, its antecedent—which is the existence of events with no beginning—is false. The explanation of the entailment: If we consider the number of events from the Flood, for example, to pre-eternity together with their number from now, for example, to pre-eternity, they would be two different numbers necessarily, and equality between them is impossible because of the realized increase in the one. And a thing without increase is not equal to itself after increase. It is also impossible for one of them to be greater than the other because of the non-finiteness (adam tanahi) of the individuals of each, so neither is exhausted by counting before the other. And the essence of the lesser (al-aqall) is what becomes finite when counted.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against the possibility of an infinite past, known as the 'proof of truncation and application' (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq). The author, Sanusi, defines it as showing the impossibility of comparing two series of events without a beginning. He structures the proof as a conditional argument: if there were events with no beginning, then there would exist two different numbers that are neither greater nor equal; this consequent is false by necessity (because any two numbers must be either equal or one greater), so the antecedent is false. He then explains the entailment: consider the number of events from a fixed past event (e.g., the Flood) to pre-eternity, and the number of events from the present to pre-eternity. These are two different numbers, and equality is impossible because the second includes the first plus additional events (increase). But neither can be greater than the other, since both are infinite (non-finite in individuals), so neither is exhausted before the other in counting. The definition of 'lesser' (al-aqall) is that which becomes finite when counted, but here neither becomes finite, so neither is actually less. Thus no relation of equality or inequality holds, which is absurd. Therefore, the assumption of an infinite past is false. This argument is a classic refutation of actual infinity in the past, using the intuitive notion that adding to an infinite set should not change its size, but here the comparison forces a contradiction.

زيادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof incorrectly denies that two infinite sets can have equal cardinality.
Correction: Sanusi is not denying Cantorian set theory; he is using the intuitive notion that adding to an infinite set should increase its size, but for actual infinities in the physical world, this leads to contradiction. The proof shows that an actual infinite past is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi identify as the impossible status between the two numbers in the proof of truncation and application?
A) They are both finite.
B) They are both odd numbers.
C) They are neither equal nor one greater than the other.
D) They are equal but with different sums.

Key Takeaway
The proof of truncation and application shows that an infinite past leads to a logical contradiction when comparing two series of events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an infinite past were possible, you would never have reached this moment. Your own existence is proof that time had a beginning. When you truly let this sink in, does it change how you view each moment of your life — as a finite gift from the One who caused it to begin? What one commitment can you make today to live with that urgency?
?
The proof forces you to accept that between any two numbers there must be equality or inequality — no third option. In your life, are there areas where you are trying to avoid a necessary decision by pretending a third, comfortable option exists? What decision about your faith or repentance are you evading right now?
?
The mutakallimun used self-evident truths like this to ground their proofs. When you reflect on the fact that your mind automatically recognizes necessary relations (like equality must hold or not), do you see this as evidence that your intellect is created and guided by the Creator? How does this compel you to trust the rational proofs of Islam more fully — and then act on them?

The Impossibility of a Beginningless Judgment Chain
Sanusi proves the falsehood of a beginningless judgment chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a judgment about past events cannot be beginningless—it would force an eternal to precede another eternal, which is impossible.

Core Idea
An eternal judgment chain would make one eternal precede another, a logical absurdity.

Sanusi begins his refutation by setting up a conditional: if judgments about past events exist, they either have a first or not. He then proves both options false. In this lesson, we examine the first option—a judgment without a beginning—and see why it leads to an impossible priority between two eternal things.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional premise
Start by reading the opening lines where Sanusi states the two possibilities for the judgment: it has a first or it does not. Notice that he claims both lead to falsehood.
2
Follow the proof for 'no first'
Read the argument that a judgment without a first requires events to precede each of its instances. Then infer that the genus of events (eternal) would precede the genus of judgments (also eternal), which is contradictory.
Key idea: one eternal cannot be prior to another.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Ask yourself: why is it impossible for one eternal thing to precede another? This is a core principle in kalam: eternity excludes priority or posteriority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكونَ لهُ أوَّل ، أو لا ، والتالي باطل بقسميه ، فالملزوم ـ وهو وجود الحكم - باطل أيضاً ، والملازمة ظاهرة
وأما بطلان التالي : فإنَّما يستبينُ ببطلان كلّ واحدٍ مِنْ قسميه ،
فنقولُ :
أما كونُ الحكم لا أَوَّلَ لَهُ فباطل ؛ لأنَّ مِنْ ضرورة هذا الحكم أنْ تسبقَ كلَّ فردٍ مِنْ أفرادِهِ حوادث ؛ ليحكم عليها بالانقضاء ، فيلزم أنْ يسبق جنسُ المحكوم عليه - وهو أزلي - جنس الحكم ؛ وهو أزلي أيضاً ، وسبق الأزلي على الأزلي محال ضرورةً .
وأما كون الحكم له أوّل فباطل أيضاً ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يُوجَدَ عددٌ متناه في نفسِهِ ، لكن زدنا عليه واحداً فصار الجميع غير متناه ! وبطلان هذا اللازم ظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّ زيادة الواحدِ على عددٍ ما زيادة شيء متناه ، والفَرْضُ أنَّ المزيدَ عليهِ متناه أيضاً ، فيكون مجموعهما متناهياً ضرورةً ، فالحكم بأنَّ المجموع غير متناه واضح البطلان
وأما بيانُ لزوم هذا المحال على تقدير انتهاء الحكم : فلنفرض مثالاً على أصلهم يتضحُ فيهِ ذلكَ ؛ وذلكَ أنْ نَفَرِضَ في حركاتِ الفلكِ مثلاً وجود حكم في يومنا هذا بانقضاء ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الحركاتِ قبلَهُ ، ثم كذلكَ حكم آخرُ في الحركة التي تلي حركة يومنا هذا قبله ، ثم هكذا توالَتِ الأحكامُ ؛ فإِنْ فُرِضَ تواليها أبداً بحيثُ لا أوَّلَ لها ، وقد عرفت أنَّ الحركاتِ المحكوم عليها بالانقضاء سابقة أبداً على الزمان الذي أيُوجَدُ فيهِ الحكم عليها . . فهو القسمُ الأَوَّلُ مِنْ قسمي التالي الذي بيَّنا أنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ سَبْقُ أزلي ـ وهو جنسُ الحوادثِ المحكوم عليها - على أزليّ ؛ وهو جنسُ الحكم عليها بالانقضاء ، وإنْ فُرِضَ أن الأحكام انقطعَتْ بحيثُ كان لها أوَّل . . فهو القسم الثاني مِنْ قسمي التالي الذي قصدنا الآنَ بيانُ بطلانِهِ
فلنفرض أنَّ تلك الأحكام توالَتْ على الوجه السابق إلى تمامِ ألفِ حركة مثلاً حُكِمَ عندَها أَنَّهُ فرغَ قبلَها مِنْ حركاتِ الفلكِ ما لا نهايةَ لَهُ ثم انقطع الحكم ، بحيثُ لم يحكم عندَ الواحدِ وألف بأنَّهُ فرغَ قبلها ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الحركاتِ ، فيلزم على هذا أن يكونَ ما قبل الواحدِ وألف مِنْ حركاتِ الفلكِ عدداً متناهياً ؛ إذ لو كان غير متناءٍ لَمَا انقطعَ الحكمُ عليه بذلك كما لم ينقطع فيما دونَهُ ، لكن قد حُكِمَ عليهِ عندَ تمامِ مجموعاً إلى الحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها.. بعدمِ النهايةِ ؛ إذِ الفَرْضُ أَنَّ أَوَّلَ الأحكام الحكم الذي وُجِدَ عندَ تمامِ الألْفِ ولا حكم قبله
فتمحَّضَ أنّ عدم النهاية المحكوم بها على مجموع الحركات التي قبل الألفِ إِنَّما جاءَ مِنَ الزيادة فيها للحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها ، بل وعدم النهاية للحركاتِ في سائر الأحكام نقولُ : إِنَّ سببَهُ
It (the judgment) either has a first or not, and the consequent is false in both its divisions, so the antecedent — which is the existence of the judgment — is also false, and the implication is clear. As for the falsehood of the consequent, it becomes clear by the falsehood of each one of its two divisions. So we say: As for the judgment having no first, it is false, because it is necessary for this judgment that events precede each of its individuals so that it judges them as having passed away, and it follows that the genus of the thing judged — which is eternal — precedes the genus of the judgment, which is also eternal, and the priority of an eternal over an eternal is impossible necessarily. As for the judgment having a first, it is also false, because it would require that a number exist that is finite in itself, but we added one to it, so the whole became infinite! And the falsehood of this necessary consequence is apparent, because adding one to a number is the addition of something finite, and the assumption is that that to which the addition is made is also finite, so their sum is necessarily finite, so the judgment that the sum is infinite is clearly false. As for the explanation of the necessity of this impossibility on the assumption of the termination of the judgment: let us assume an example according to their principle to clarify that. That is, let us assume in the motions of the celestial sphere, for example, the existence of a judgment on this day of ours concerning the passing away of an infinite number of motions before it, then similarly another judgment concerning the motion that precedes the motion of this day of ours, then thus the judgments follow in succession. If their succession is assumed to be eternal such that they have no first, and you have known that the motions that are judged to have passed away are forever prior to the time in which the judgment about them exists — this is the first of the two divisions of the consequent whose impossibility we have explained, namely that an eternal — being the genus of events judged — would precede an eternal — being the genus of the judgment about them as passing away. And if it is assumed that the judgments ceased such that they had a first — this is the second division of the consequent which we now intend to explain its falsehood. So let us assume that those judgments followed in the previous manner up to the completion of a thousand motions, for example; at it a judgment is made that before them an infinite [number] of the motions of the celestial sphere have finished, then the judgment ceased, so that at one thousand and one it is not judged that before it an infinite number of motions have finished. It follows from this that what is before one thousand and one of the motions of the celestial sphere is a finite number, because if it were infinite, the judgment about it would not have ceased as it did not cease for what is less than it. But it has been judged about it at the completion of the sum (including) the one motion that follows the thousand before it, [as having] no end, because the assumption is that the first of the judgments is the judgment that exists at the completion of the thousand, and no judgment before it. It became clear that the lack of infinity attributed to the sum of the motions before the thousand came only from the addition to them of the one motion that follows the thousand before it. Rather, the lack of infinity for the motions in the rest of the judgments, we say: its cause is...
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a complete refutation argument. It begins with a conditional statement: if a judgment about past events exists, then it either has a first (i.e., it began at some point) or it does not (i.e., it is beginningless). The author, Sanusi, states that the consequent (the judgment having either state) is false in both its divisions, so the antecedent (the existence of such a judgment) must also be false. He then proves the falsehood of each division in turn.

First, he shows that a judgment without a first is impossible. For a judgment to judge that events have passed away, those events must precede the judgment. If the judgment has no beginning, then an infinite number of events would precede it. The genus of those events (the set of all past events) would be eternal, and the genus of the judgments would also be eternal. But one eternal thing cannot precede another eternal thing, as that would imply a priority that contradicts their coeternity. This is a contradiction, so a judgment without a first is impossible.

Second, he shows that a judgment with a first is also impossible. If the judgment began at a specific moment, then consider the set of all such judgments up to that moment. This set would be finite (since it began). But if we add one more judgment after it, the total would become infinite, which is absurd because adding a finite number to a finite number yields a finite number. This is a direct contradiction: increasing a finite number by one cannot produce infinity. Therefore, a judgment with a first is also impossible.

Sanusi then clarifies the impossibility by an example from the motions of the celestial sphere. Suppose we have a judgment today that an infinite number of past motions have passed away, then another judgment about the motion before today, and so on in succession. If this series of judgments has no beginning, we fall into the first absurdity (eternal preceding eternal). If the series stops at some point (e.g., after a thousand motions, we judge that an infinite number preceded, but then we stop judging), then the motions before the thousand plus one are finite because if they were infinite, the judgment would not have stopped. But since we did judge that the sum up to a thousand is infinite, and then we added one motion, the claim that the sum becomes infinite is false; the only way it could seem infinite is due to the addition of that one finite motion, which is clearly insufficient. This confirms the second absurdity.

Thus, both possible states of the judgment lead to impossibility, so the assumption that such a judgment exists at all must be rejected. This argument is a standard kalam proof for the creation of the world, showing that an infinite regress of judgments or events is impossible.

Key Terms
حكم
أزلي
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an eternal chain of judgments is possible if we imagine an infinite past.
Correction: Sanusi shows that such an eternal chain would require one eternal (events) to be prior to another eternal (judgments), which is logically impossible because eternals cannot have priority relationships.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi conclude from the falsehood of a judgment without a first?
A) Such a judgment is possible but requires careful reasoning.
B) Such a judgment is impossible because it forces an eternal to precede another eternal.
C) Such a judgment is impossible because events cannot be eternal.
D) Such a judgment is possible only if the events are also beginningless.

Key Takeaway
A beginningless chain of judgments about past events is impossible because it would contradict the nature of eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's knowledge of every past event, have you ever pictured it as a long sequence of judgments stretching back infinitely? How does Sanusi's argument challenge that picture? Will you now affirm that God's knowledge is a simple eternal act, not a series?
?
If a beginningless chain of judgments is impossible, what does that imply for how you understand your own actions and God's recording of them? Does this bring you to rely more on the eternal, all-encompassing nature of God's knowledge rather than on temporal sequences?
?
Sanusi proves that one eternal cannot precede another. Do you ever allow your mind to imagine God's attributes as having priority or sequence? How can you train yourself to avoid such imaginative projections and accept the logical necessity of divine simplicity?

The Impossibility of a Judgment Chain with a Beginning
Sanusi proves the falsehood of a judgment chain with a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a judgment about past events cannot have a beginning—it would make a finite set become infinite by adding one, an absurdity.

Core Idea
A judgment chain with a first would turn a finite number into an infinite by adding one, a clear contradiction.

Having disproved the beginningless option, Sanusi now tackles the second: a judgment that has a first (a beginning). He shows that if the chain begins, then the total number of judgments up to that point is finite, but adding one more judgment would supposedly make the whole infinite—an obvious logical fallacy. He then illustrates with a concrete example from celestial motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Start with 'As for the judgment having a first' and follow the argument that if the chain has a first, then the number of judgments is finite, but adding one would make it infinite, which is impossible.
2
Understand the example
Read the example about motions of the celestial sphere. Sanusi assumes judgments occur about each motion, and if they stop after a thousand, then the motions before that point become finite, yet the judgment claimed them infinite.
This illustrates the contradiction concretely.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The example shows that the only way to avoid the first absurdity is to have a beginning, but that leads to the second absurdity (finite becoming infinite). Therefore, no such judgment chain can exist.

وأما بطلان التالي : فإنَّما يستبينُ ببطلان كلّ واحدٍ مِنْ قسميه ،
فنقولُ :
أما كونُ الحكم لا أَوَّلَ لَهُ فباطل ؛ لأنَّ مِنْ ضرورة هذا الحكم أنْ تسبقَ كلَّ فردٍ مِنْ أفرادِهِ حوادث ؛ ليحكم عليها بالانقضاء ، فيلزم أنْ يسبق جنسُ المحكوم عليه - وهو أزلي - جنس الحكم ؛ وهو أزلي أيضاً ، وسبق الأزلي على الأزلي محال ضرورةً .
وأما كون الحكم له أوّل فباطل أيضاً ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يُوجَدَ عددٌ متناه في نفسِهِ ، لكن زدنا عليه واحداً فصار الجميع غير متناه ! وبطلان هذا اللازم ظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّ زيادة الواحدِ على عددٍ ما زيادة شيء متناه ، والفَرْضُ أنَّ المزيدَ عليهِ متناه أيضاً ، فيكون مجموعهما متناهياً ضرورةً ، فالحكم بأنَّ المجموع غير متناه واضح البطلان
وأما بيانُ لزوم هذا المحال على تقدير انتهاء الحكم : فلنفرض مثالاً على أصلهم يتضحُ فيهِ ذلكَ ؛ وذلكَ أنْ نَفَرِضَ في حركاتِ الفلكِ مثلاً وجود حكم في يومنا هذا بانقضاء ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الحركاتِ قبلَهُ ، ثم كذلكَ حكم آخرُ في الحركة التي تلي حركة يومنا هذا قبله ، ثم هكذا توالَتِ الأحكامُ ؛ فإِنْ فُرِضَ تواليها أبداً بحيثُ لا أوَّلَ لها ، وقد عرفت أنَّ الحركاتِ المحكوم عليها بالانقضاء سابقة أبداً على الزمان الذي أيُوجَدُ فيهِ الحكم عليها . . فهو القسمُ الأَوَّلُ مِنْ قسمي التالي الذي بيَّنا أنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ سَبْقُ أزلي ـ وهو جنسُ الحوادثِ المحكوم عليها - على أزليّ ؛ وهو جنسُ الحكم عليها بالانقضاء ، وإنْ فُرِضَ أن الأحكام انقطعَتْ بحيثُ كان لها أوَّل . . فهو القسم الثاني مِنْ قسمي التالي الذي قصدنا الآنَ بيانُ بطلانِهِ
فلنفرض أنَّ تلك الأحكام توالَتْ على الوجه السابق إلى تمامِ ألفِ حركة مثلاً حُكِمَ عندَها أَنَّهُ فرغَ قبلَها مِنْ حركاتِ الفلكِ ما لا نهايةَ لَهُ ثم انقطع الحكم ، بحيثُ لم يحكم عندَ الواحدِ وألف بأنَّهُ فرغَ قبلها ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الحركاتِ ، فيلزم على هذا أن يكونَ ما قبل الواحدِ وألف مِنْ حركاتِ الفلكِ عدداً متناهياً ؛ إذ لو كان غير متناءٍ لَمَا انقطعَ الحكمُ عليه بذلك كما لم ينقطع فيما دونَهُ ، لكن قد حُكِمَ عليهِ عندَ تمامِ مجموعاً إلى الحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها.. بعدمِ النهايةِ ؛ إذِ الفَرْضُ أَنَّ أَوَّلَ الأحكام الحكم الذي وُجِدَ عندَ تمامِ الألْفِ ولا حكم قبله
فتمحَّضَ أنّ عدم النهاية المحكوم بها على مجموع الحركات التي قبل الألفِ إِنَّما جاءَ مِنَ الزيادة فيها للحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها ، بل وعدم النهاية للحركاتِ في سائر الأحكام نقولُ : إِنَّ سببَهُ
It (the judgment) either has a first or not, and the consequent is false in both its divisions, so the antecedent — which is the existence of the judgment — is also false, and the implication is clear. As for the falsehood of the consequent, it becomes clear by the falsehood of each one of its two divisions. So we say: As for the judgment having no first, it is false, because it is necessary for this judgment that events precede each of its individuals so that it judges them as having passed away, and it follows that the genus of the thing judged — which is eternal — precedes the genus of the judgment, which is also eternal, and the priority of an eternal over an eternal is impossible necessarily. As for the judgment having a first, it is also false, because it would require that a number exist that is finite in itself, but we added one to it, so the whole became infinite! And the falsehood of this necessary consequence is apparent, because adding one to a number is the addition of something finite, and the assumption is that that to which the addition is made is also finite, so their sum is necessarily finite, so the judgment that the sum is infinite is clearly false. As for the explanation of the necessity of this impossibility on the assumption of the termination of the judgment: let us assume an example according to their principle to clarify that. That is, let us assume in the motions of the celestial sphere, for example, the existence of a judgment on this day of ours concerning the passing away of an infinite number of motions before it, then similarly another judgment concerning the motion that precedes the motion of this day of ours, then thus the judgments follow in succession. If their succession is assumed to be eternal such that they have no first, and you have known that the motions that are judged to have passed away are forever prior to the time in which the judgment about them exists — this is the first of the two divisions of the consequent whose impossibility we have explained, namely that an eternal — being the genus of events judged — would precede an eternal — being the genus of the judgment about them as passing away. And if it is assumed that the judgments ceased such that they had a first — this is the second division of the consequent which we now intend to explain its falsehood. So let us assume that those judgments followed in the previous manner up to the completion of a thousand motions, for example; at it a judgment is made that before them an infinite [number] of the motions of the celestial sphere have finished, then the judgment ceased, so that at one thousand and one it is not judged that before it an infinite number of motions have finished. It follows from this that what is before one thousand and one of the motions of the celestial sphere is a finite number, because if it were infinite, the judgment about it would not have ceased as it did not cease for what is less than it. But it has been judged about it at the completion of the sum (including) the one motion that follows the thousand before it, [as having] no end, because the assumption is that the first of the judgments is the judgment that exists at the completion of the thousand, and no judgment before it. It became clear that the lack of infinity attributed to the sum of the motions before the thousand came only from the addition to them of the one motion that follows the thousand before it. Rather, the lack of infinity for the motions in the rest of the judgments, we say: its cause is...
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a complete refutation argument. It begins with a conditional statement: if a judgment about past events exists, then it either has a first (i.e., it began at some point) or it does not (i.e., it is beginningless). The author, Sanusi, states that the consequent (the judgment having either state) is false in both its divisions, so the antecedent (the existence of such a judgment) must also be false. He then proves the falsehood of each division in turn.

First, he shows that a judgment without a first is impossible. For a judgment to judge that events have passed away, those events must precede the judgment. If the judgment has no beginning, then an infinite number of events would precede it. The genus of those events (the set of all past events) would be eternal, and the genus of the judgments would also be eternal. But one eternal thing cannot precede another eternal thing, as that would imply a priority that contradicts their coeternity. This is a contradiction, so a judgment without a first is impossible.

Second, he shows that a judgment with a first is also impossible. If the judgment began at a specific moment, then consider the set of all such judgments up to that moment. This set would be finite (since it began). But if we add one more judgment after it, the total would become infinite, which is absurd because adding a finite number to a finite number yields a finite number. This is a direct contradiction: increasing a finite number by one cannot produce infinity. Therefore, a judgment with a first is also impossible.

Sanusi then clarifies the impossibility by an example from the motions of the celestial sphere. Suppose we have a judgment today that an infinite number of past motions have passed away, then another judgment about the motion before today, and so on in succession. If this series of judgments has no beginning, we fall into the first absurdity (eternal preceding eternal). If the series stops at some point (e.g., after a thousand motions, we judge that an infinite number preceded, but then we stop judging), then the motions before the thousand plus one are finite because if they were infinite, the judgment would not have stopped. But since we did judge that the sum up to a thousand is infinite, and then we added one motion, the claim that the sum becomes infinite is false; the only way it could seem infinite is due to the addition of that one finite motion, which is clearly insufficient. This confirms the second absurdity.

Thus, both possible states of the judgment lead to impossibility, so the assumption that such a judgment exists at all must be rejected. This argument is a standard kalam proof for the creation of the world, showing that an infinite regress of judgments or events is impossible.

متناه
غير متناه
لازم
تسلسل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a judgment chain has a beginning, it is unproblematic because we can count the judgments.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even with a beginning, the chain would involve an impossible transformation of a finite number into an infinite by adding one, which is logically invalid.

Checkpoint

A) Adding one finite number to another finite number results in an infinite number.
B) A finite set of judgments cannot cover an infinite past.
C) The judgments would need to be eternal to cover infinite events.
D) The example shows that judgments cannot exist at all.

Key Takeaway
A judgment chain with a beginning is impossible because it would require a finite number to become infinite by adding one, an obvious absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself thinking 'just this one small sin won't matter,' consider Sanusi's logic: adding one finite thing to a finite set of sins cannot change its fundamental nature. But does the accumulation of sins truly remain finite in the sight of Allah? How does this realization press you to abandon the 'just one more' mentality starting today?
?
You may believe that a single good deed is finite, and that you need an infinite number to attain closeness to Allah. But Sanusi's argument shows that adding finite to finite never yields infinite. So how can your finite deeds ever bridge the gap? Does this not force you to rely solely on Allah's infinite mercy, and to commit to seeking His forgiveness daily, rather than trusting in your own record?

Proof that Past Events Cannot Be Infinite
Sanusi presents a reductio argument against infinite past events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument proving that an actual infinite regress of past events is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Adding one finite to a finite cannot produce an actual infinite.

Sanusi presents a sophisticated reductio argument to demonstrate that past events cannot be infinite in number. He constructs a hypothetical scenario involving sequential judgments about motions to expose a contradiction if past events were infinite. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context: motions and judgments
The argument deals with motions of the sphere (celestial motions) that are judged to be passing away. These judgments are sequential and prior to the time of judgment.
This sets up a framework of successive events and judgments.
2
Follow the hypothetical: two divisions of the consequent
Sanusi first recalls that motions judged to be passing away are always prior to the judgment. He then outlines two possibilities: either the judgments (like the motions) are infinite, or they have a beginning. He aims to show the latter leads to absurdity.
3
See the reductio: finite set becomes infinite by adding one motion
Sanusi constructs a specific scenario: judgments occur up to a thousand motions, and at that point it is judged that before them there were infinite motions. Then the judgment ceases at the thousand-first motion. This leads to a contradiction where a finite set (motions before the thousand-first) would have to be infinite because of the earlier judgment.
The key point: if the motions before the thousand-first were infinite, the judgment of infinity would not have ceased. But it did cease, so they are finite. Yet the earlier judgment declared the sum (including the thousand-first?) infinite. Thus, adding a single finite motion to a finite set would produce infinity, which is absurd.
4
Generalize the conclusion
The same reasoning applies to any claim of beginningless events. The argument shows that an actual infinite regress is impossible, confirming that past events must have a beginning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا أوَّلَ لها ، وقد عرفت أنَّ الحركاتِ المحكوم عليها بالانقضاء سابقة أبداً على الزمان الذي يُوجَدُ فيه الحكم عليها . . فهو القسمُ الأَوَّلُ مِنْ قسمي التالي الذي بيَّنا أنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ سَبْقُ أزلي ـ وهو جنسُ الحوادثِ المحكوم عليها - على أزليّ ؛ وهو جنسُ الحكم عليها بالانقضاء ، وإنْ فُرِضَ أن الأحكام انقطعتْ بحيثُ كان لها أوَّل . . فهو القسم الثاني مِنْ قسمي التالي الذي قصدنا الآنَ بيانُ بطلانِهِ. فلنفرض أنَّ تلك الأحكام توالَتْ على الوجه السابق إلى تمامِ ألفِ حركة مثلاً حُكِمَ عندَها أَنَّهُ فرغَ قبلَها مِنْ حركاتِ الفلكِ ما لا نهايةَ لَهُ ثم انقطع الحكم ، بحيثُ لم يحكم عندَ الواحدِ وألف بأنَّهُ فرغَ قبلها ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الحركاتِ ، فيلزم على هذا أن يكونَ ما قبل الواحدِ وألف مِنْ حركاتِ الفلكِ عدداً متناهياً ؛ إذ لو كان غير متناهٍ لَمَا انقطعَ الحكمُ عليه بذلك كما لم ينقطع فيما دونَهُ ، لكن قد حُكِمَ عليهِ عندَ تمامِ مجموعاً إلى الحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها.. بعدمِ النهايةِ ؛ إذِ الفَرْضُ أَنَّ أَوَّلَ الأحكام الحكم الذي وُجِدَ عندَ تمامِ الألْفِ ولا حكم قبله. فتمحَّضَ أنّ عدم النهاية المحكوم بها على مجموع الحركات التي قبل الألفِ إِنَّما جاءَ مِنَ الزيادة فيها للحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها ، بل وعدم النهاية للحركاتِ في سائر الأحكام نقولُ : إِنَّ سببَهُ زيادة هذه الحركةِ الواحدة فيها ؛ لأنَّ ما قبل هذه الحركة متناه ، وإلا لَوُجِدَ الحكم عليه بعدم النهايةِ ، والفَرْضُ انقطاعه ، وما بعدها متناه أيضاً ؛ إذ أعلاه ألف حركة ، ولا ريبَ أَنَّها متناهيةٌ . فإذاً ؛ لا سبب لعدم النهاية في جميع الأحكام إلا زيادة تلكَ الحركةِ الواحدة ، فقد لزمَ أنَّ ما يتناهى ؛ وهو ما قبل تلك الحركة الواحدةِ وما بعدَها مِنَ الحركاتِ .. صارَ لا يتناهى بسبب زيادة حركة واحدة فيه ؛ وهي الحركةُ التي تلي الألف قبلَها ، وإِن شئتَ فاقتصر على ذكر ما قبل هذه الحركة فإنَّهُ يتناهى ، وقد صار لا يتناهى عند زيادة تلك الحركةِ عليهِ ، وهو أقرب وأظهرُ ، والله أعلم . ولا يخفى عليك إجراء مثل هذا في سائر ما قالوا بهِ مِنْ حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وبعد هذا البيانِ لا يبقى عليك إشكال في لفظ العقيدة ، وبالله التوفيق ، ولا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا بالله .
They have no beginning, and you have known that the motions judged to be passing away are always prior to the time in which the judgment upon them occurs. So that is the first of the two divisions of the consequent which we explained necessitates the priority of an eternal — which is the genus of the accidents judged — over an eternal — which is the genus of the judgment upon them of passing away. And if it is supposed that the judgments ceased such that they had a beginning, then that is the second of the two divisions of the consequent which we now intend to show its falsehood. So let us suppose that those judgments followed one another in the previous manner up to the completion of, for example, a thousand motions; it is judged at that point that before them there had passed of the motions of the sphere what has no end; then the judgment ceased, such that at (motion) one-thousand-and-one it is not judged that before it there had passed what has no end of motions. Then it would follow from this that what is before one-thousand-and-one of the motions of the sphere is a finite number; for if it were infinite, the judgment upon it of that would not have ceased just as it did not cease for what is below it. But it has been judged upon it at the completion of the sum up to the single motion that follows the thousand before it as having no end; since the assumption is that the first of the judgments is the judgment that occurred at the completion of the thousand, and no judgment before it. So it becomes evident that the lack of end judged upon the sum of motions before the thousand only came from the increase in it of the single motion that follows the thousand before it. Rather, and the lack of end for the motions in all the judgments — we say: its cause is the increase of this single motion in them; because what is before this motion is finite, otherwise the judgment upon it of lack of end would have been found, and the assumption is its cessation, and what is after it is also finite; since its upper limit is a thousand motions, and there is no doubt that they are finite. So then: there is no cause for the lack of end in all the judgments except the increase of that single motion. So it has become necessary that what is finite — which is what is before that single motion and what is after it of motions — becomes infinite due to the increase of a single motion in it; and that is the motion that follows the thousand before it. And if you wish, restrict yourself to mentioning what is before this motion, for it is finite, and it became infinite when that motion was added to it, and that is nearer and more apparent. And God knows best. And it is not hidden from you that like this is to be applied to all that they have said of events that have no beginning. And after this explanation, no difficulty remains for you regarding the wording of the creed. And with God is success. And there is no might nor power except with God.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage contains a reductio ad absurdum proof that past events (motions of the sphere) cannot be infinite. The author begins by recalling that motions judged to be passing away are always prior to the time of the judgment upon them, establishing a context of sequential judgments. He then presents the two possible divisions of the consequent: either the judgments (like the motions) are infinite (first division), or they have a beginning (second division). He aims to prove the second division false. He then constructs a hypothetical: suppose judgments occur up to a thousand motions, and at the thousandth motion it is judged that before it there were infinite motions. Then the judgment stops at the thousand-first motion, such that no judgment of infinity is made at that point. From this, it follows that the motions before the thousand-first are finite — otherwise the judgment of infinity would not have ceased. Yet the judgment at the thousandth motion had already declared the sum of motions (including the one just after the thousandth?) to be infinite. Careful: the Arabic says 'at the completion of the sum up to the single motion that follows the thousand before it' — i.e., at the thousand-and-oneth motion, but the judgment there is that the motions before it (including that motion?) are infinite? Actually the text is dense: 'حُكِمَ عليهِ عندَ تمامِ مجموعاً إلى الحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها بعدمِ النهاية' — 'it was judged at the completion of the sum up to the single motion that follows the thousand before it as having no end.' This indicates that the same judgment of infinity is applied to the set that includes the thousand-first motion? No, the assumption is that the first judgment is at the thousandth, so the judgment at the thousand-first is absent. But earlier it says 'حُكِمَ عليها' at the thousandth that before it there were infinite motions. Then it says 'then the judgment ceased' and at the thousand-first no judgment. Then it argues that the motions before the thousand-first must be finite, else the judgment would not have ceased. But then it notes that the judgment at the thousandth had already declared the sum (which includes the motion before the thousandth? Actually the sum includes motions up to the thousandth? The text becomes clearer later. The key conclusion: the infinity of the sum of motions before the thousandth is attributed to the addition of a single motion (the one that follows the thousandth? Actually it says 'الحركة الواحدة التي تلي الألف قبلها' — the single motion that follows the thousand before it. That motion is the one-thousand-and-first? Or a motion before the thousand? The phrase 'تلي الألف قبلها' means 'that follows the thousand before it' — ambiguous. But based on the standard kalam argument, the idea is that if you take a finite set (e.g., motions before motion X) and add one motion, you cannot get an infinite set. So the author concludes: the cause of infinity in all judgments is the addition of that single motion, but since what is before and after it are finite, adding a finite to a finite cannot yield infinite, so the premise is false. Hence past events must be finite. He ends by noting this applies to all claims of beginningless events, and that this resolves any ambiguity in the creedal statement.

Key Terms
حركات (motions)
حكم (judgment)
انقضاء (passing away)
أزلي (eternal)
متناهٍ (finite)
غير متناهٍ (infinite)
عدم النهاية (infinity)
فلك (sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is based on empirical observation of celestial motions.
Correction: The argument is purely rational and logical, not empirical. It demonstrates a contradiction in the concept of an actual infinite regress, regardless of physical motions.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's argument, what is the cause of the judgment of infinity for the motions before the thousandth?
A) The motions themselves are intrinsically infinite.
B) The addition of a single motion to a finite set.
C) The fact that judgments never cease.
D) The nature of the celestial sphere.

Key Takeaway
This argument refutes the possibility of an actual infinite regress of past events, affirming that the world had a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen a logical proof that the world had a beginning. If that beginning was initiated by a Creator, what does that mean for how you treat each moment of your finite life? Are you living with the urgency that this truth demands, or are you acting as if time will never run out?
?
The argument shows that adding finite to finite can never produce infinite. How does this truth confront your tendency to think you have 'more time' to repent or to seek God? If each day is finite, what must you do today that you are putting off?
?
If the past is finite and there is a beginning, then you are accountable to the One who started it all. Does your daily schedule, your worries, your priorities reflect that you actually believe this? What is one specific change you can make this week to align your life with the reality that the world had a beginning?
