Proof via Concomitance and Refuting the Objection about Permissibility
Sanusi presents the proof via concomitance and refutes an objection about permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the logical principle of concomitance to prove that attributes of meanings are causes of moral attributes, and how he refutes the objection that permissibility is required for causation.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence in kalam reasoning.

Sanusi continues his defense of divine attributes by addressing a critical objection to his proof via concomitance. Opponents argue that in the witnessed world, causation requires permissibility, which does not apply to God. This lesson unpacks Sanusi's response, revealing a key logical fallacy and demonstrating the strength of the original proof.

Guided Path
1
Review the Proof via Concomitance
Recap that Sanusi earlier established a necessary connection (تلازم) between the seven moral attributes and the attributes of meanings. Understand that this concomitance serves as the foundation for the proof.
This proof relies on the analogy between the witnessed world (شاهد) and the unseen world (غائب).
2
Identify the Objection
The opponents claim that in the witnessed world, rulings are caused only because permissibility (جواز) exists, and since permissibility is absent in God's judgments, the analogy fails. Notice they are attempting to reverse the proof.
They argue that if permissibility is absent, causation must also be absent.
3
Analyze Sanusi's Refutation
Sanusi points out two fallacies: First, reversing the proof (عكس الدليل) is not binding. Second, invalidating the reverse of the cause (عكس العلة) is actually binding against them. He explains that permissibility is merely evidence of causation, so its absence does not prove the absence of causation.
This is the key logical point: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.
4
Spot the Inconsistency
The opponents affirm the effects (moral attributes) while denying the causes (attributes of meanings) in God. This inconsistency shows they have 'reversed reason' (عكسوا المعقول).
If the cause is absent, the effect should be absent too, but they inconsistently maintain the effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى البرهان بهذا الطريق - وهو طريقُ التلازم - أشرتُ بقولي : ( إما لتحققِ تلازمهما في الشاهدِ ) أي : تلازم الأوصاف السبع المعنوية وصفاتِ المعاني ، وقد عرفت فيما مضى تفسيرهما ، والمجرور - وهو قولي : ( لتحقق ) - يتعلق بالفعل مِنْ قولي قبلُ : ) تلازمها معان ) . وقولهم : إِنَّ الأحكامَ إِنَّما عُلِّلَتْ في الشاهد لجوازها ، والجواز منتف في أحكامِهِ تعالى .. إلزام منهم لعكس الدليل ؛ وهو لا يُلزم ، وإبطال لعكس العلة ، وهو لازم ؛ فإِنَّ الجواز في الشاهدِ دليل على تعليل الأحكام المعنويَّة بمعانيها ، فلا يلزَمُ مِنْ عدمِهِ في حقٌّ الغائب عدم المدلول الذي هو التعليلُ ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يلزَمُ مِنْ عدمِ الدليل عدم المدلول ، وهم قالوا : يلزَمُ ذلكَ ، وصفاتُ المعاني علل للصفات المعنويَّةِ ، فيلزَمُ مِنْ عدمها عدمُها ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزَمُ مِنْ عدم العلَّةِ عدم المعلول ، وهم قالوا بعدم لزوم ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّهم نَفَوا في حق الغائبِ صفاتِ المعاني ، وأثبتوا معلولاتها ؛ وهي الصفات المعنوية ، فقد عكسوا المعقول
And to the proof by this way - which is the way of concomitance - I indicated by my saying: 'Either because of the verification of their concomitance in the witnessed world,' i.e., the concomitance of the seven moral attributes and the attributes of meanings. And you have already known their interpretation in what preceded. And the prepositional phrase - which is my saying 'for verification' - is attached to the verb from my saying earlier: 'their concomitance with meanings.' And their saying: 'Verily, rulings are only caused in the witnessed world due to their permissibility, and permissibility is absent in His judgments, may He be exalted...' is a binding of them to the reverse of the proof, but it is not binding, and an invalidation of the reverse of the cause, which is binding. For verily, permissibility in the witnessed world is evidence of the causation of moral attributes by their meanings. So the absence of it in the case of the absent does not necessitate the absence of the indicated meaning which is causation, because the absence of evidence does not necessitate the absence of what is indicated. And they said that it does necessitate that, and the attributes of meanings are causes for the moral attributes, so the absence of them necessitates their absence, because the absence of the cause necessitates the absence of the effect. And they said that it is not necessary, because they negated the attributes of meanings in the case of the absent but affirmed their effects, which are the moral attributes. So they have inverted reason.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the proof via concomitance (تلازم) and then refutes an objection. Sanusi begins by referencing his earlier statement about the proof: it relies on the established concomitance between the seven moral attributes (الصفات المعنوية) and the attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني). He clarifies the grammatical structure for clarity. Then he quotes the objection: the opponents argue that in the witnessed world, rulings are only caused because of permissibility (جواز), but permissibility is absent in God's judgments, so the analogy fails. Sanusi responds by saying this is actually an attempt to reverse the proof (عكس الدليل) and invalidate the reverse of the cause, which backfires. He explains: permissibility in the witnessed world is evidence that moral attributes are caused by meanings, but the absence of this evidence (permissibility) in the divine case does not prove the absence of causation, because lack of evidence is not evidence of lack. The opponents claim that if the cause (attribute of meaning) is absent, the effect (moral attribute) must be absent; but they themselves, regarding God, affirm the effects (moral attributes) while denying the causes (attributes of meanings). This inconsistency, Sanusi concludes, shows they have reversed reason.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If permissibility is absent in God, then causation cannot exist.
Correction: Sanusi explains that permissibility is merely evidence of causation; its absence does not prove the absence of the causation itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with the objection that permissibility is a condition for causation in the witnessed world, so it must also apply to God?
A) It forces a contradiction in God's attributes.
B) It assumes that what is true in the witnessed world must be true for God without evidence.
C) It confuses the absence of evidence of causation with evidence of absence of causation.
D) It denies the possibility of miracles.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that logical consistency requires accepting causes for the effects they produce, and that failing to find evidence does not disprove the relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever affirm good qualities in yourself or others while denying the means that produce them? For example, do you wish for deep faith without consistent worship, or for wisdom without seeking knowledge? How does this lesson's logic call you to be honest about causes and effects in your spiritual journey? What concrete step can you take today to align your beliefs with consistent practice?
?
The opponents inverted reason by accepting the effect while denying the cause. Is there any belief you hold about Allah that is logically inconsistent? Perhaps you affirm His mercy but doubt His justice, or accept His power but question His wisdom. What would it mean for you to submit your intellect fully to the consistency of divine attributes as taught by the scholars?
?
Sanusi uses the principle 'absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.' When have you judged something absent from your life—such as divine mercy, guidance, or forgiveness—simply because you did not see clear evidence? How might this lesson challenge you to reconsider your perspective and search for hidden causes that are actually present?

The Doctrine That Allah's Essence Is Unknown
Statement of the doctrine and its scholarly support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thesis that Allah's essence is unknown to humans, the scholars who upheld it, and the subsidiary question of whether this applies absolutely.

Core Idea
Allah's essence is unknown even to the greatest scholars.

Sanusi begins by asserting that when we realize the most specific attribute of Allah is unknowable, we must conclude His essence is unknown. He then names major scholars who held this view and notes an exception. A subsidiary question arises: is this unknowability absolute or only in this life?

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis statement
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states that the most specific attribute of Allah is unknown, leading to the conclusion that His essence is unknown. Notice the phrase 'al-aṣaḥḥ' (the sounder view).
2
Identify the proponents
Note the scholars al-Qāḍī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn, Ḥujjat al-Islām, and Imām al-Fakhr (al-Rāzī) as supporters. Also note that al-Rāzī in his early work held the opposite view.
This shows there is scholarly debate.
3
Understand the subsidiary question
Read the question: is the denial of knowledge absolute (including the Hereafter) or only in this world? Note the different positions reported from al-Imām, al-Ghazālī, al-Qāḍī, and Ḍirār.
This nuance is important for the full doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : وإذا تبيَّنَ لكَ أنَّ أخصَّ وصْفِ الباري جلَّ وعلا مجهول . . عرفتَ أنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى غيرُ معروفة للبشرِ ، وهو الأصحُ مِنَ القولين وإليه ذهب القاضي وإمام الحرمين وحُجَّةُ الإسلام والإمام الفخرُ في أكثرِ كتبِهِ ، واختارَ في كتاب ( الإشارة ) ـ وهو مِنْ أَوَّلِ مُصنَّفَاتِهِ - أنَّها معلومة ، وعلى المنع : فهل هو مطلقاً ولو في الآخرة ، أو إنَّما في الحال ، ويجوز أن تصير معلومة بعد ؟ نقل سيفُ الدينِ عنِ الإمامِ والغزالي المنع مطلقاً ، ونقل فيهِ الوقف عن القاضي وضرار واحتجَّ مَنْ قالَ بأنَّ حقيقةَ الذاتِ الكريمة معلومة : بما تقدَّمَ مِنْ جواب موسى عليه السلام لفرعون حين سألَهُ عن الحقيقة ، وقد سبق ردُّه ، واحتجوا أيضاً : بأنَّا نحكم على الذات العليَّةِ بأحكام ، والحكم على الشيء فرعُ معرفتِهِ ، وهو مردودٌ بأنَّ الحكم على الشيء فرع الشعور به بوجه ما ، ولو بوجه خارجي إجمالي ، لا فرع معرفةِ ذاتِهِ التي هي محل النزاع واحتج القائلون بأنَّها غير معلومة بالمنقول والمعقول : أما المنقولُ : فقوله تعالى : ( وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه : ١١٠] ، وقوله : ( لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام : (۱۰۳) على وجه ، وقد قيل : إنَّما سُمِّيَ : ( الله ) مِنْ وَلَهِ العقولِ وتحيرها في كُنْهِ جلالِهِ تعالى . وبالجملة : فعجْزُ العقولِ عنِ الإحاطة بعظيم كبريائه جلَّ وعلا وباهر جماله وعلي جلالِهِ ، بل عجزها عن عجائب صُنْعِهِ في مخلوقاته .. يكاد أن يكونَ معلوماً مِنَ الدين ضرورةً . وأما المعقولُ : فقال الإمام فخر الدين : ( الدليل عليهِ : أَنَّ المعلوم عندَ البشرِ أمورٌ أربعةُ : إمَّا الوجود ، وإمَّا كيفيات الوجودِ ؛ الأزليَّة والأبدية والوجوب ، وإمَّا السُّلوبُ ؛ وإمَّا السُّلوبُ ؛ وهي أَنَّهُ ليسَ بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ، وإمَّا الإضافية ؛ وهي العالميَّةُ والقادرية ، والذات المخصوصة الموصوفة بهذه المفهوماتِ مغايرة لها لا محالة ، وليسَ عندَنا مِنْ تلكَ الذاتِ المخصوصة إلا أنَّها ذاتُ لا ندري ما هي إلا أنَّها موصوفةٌ بهذهِ الصفاتِ ، وهذا يدلُّ على أنَّ ذاته المخصوصة غير معلومة
I say: When it has become clear to you that the most specific attribute of the Creator, majestic and exalted, is unknown... you know that His essence, exalted is He, is unknown to humans, and this is the sounder of the two views. To it went al-Qāḍī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn, Ḥujjat al-Islām, and Imām al-Fakhr in most of his books. He chose in the book 'al-Ishāra' – which is among his earliest works – that it is known. Regarding the denial: is it absolute, even in the Hereafter, or only in the present state, and it may become known afterwards? Sayf al-Dīn narrated from al-Imām and al-Ghazālī the absolute denial, and he narrated from al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār that they stopped. And those who said that the reality of the noble essence is known argued with the previously mentioned answer of Moses, peace be upon him, to Pharaoh when he asked about the reality, and its refutation has already preceded. They also argued that we make judgments about the supreme essence, and judgment about a thing is a branch of knowledge of it. This is refuted because judgment about a thing is a branch of awareness of it in some way, even if by a general external aspect, not a branch of knowledge of its essence, which is the point of contention. And those who say it is unknown argued by transmitted and rational [proofs]. As for the transmitted: His saying, exalted: 'and they encompass Him not in knowledge' [Ṭā Hā 110], and His saying: 'Eyesight comprehends Him not' [al-Anʿām 103] in one sense. And it has been said: He was named 'Allāh' only from the bewilderment of intellects and their perplexity at the reality of His majesty, exalted. And in summary: the incapacity of intellects to encompass His great majesty, glorious and exalted, and His dazzling beauty and lofty sublimity, rather their incapacity regarding the wonders of His handiwork in His creation... is almost known from religion by necessity. As for the rational [proof], Imām Fakhr al-Dīn said: 'The evidence for it is that what is known to humans is four things: either existence, or modes of existence: eternity, perpetuity, and necessity; or negations: that He is not a body, not a substance, not an accident; or relative attributes: being all-knowing and all-powerful. And the specific essence described by these concepts is necessarily distinct from them. And we have nothing of that specific essence except that it is an essence we do not know what it is except that it is described by these attributes. And this indicates that His specific essence is unknown.'
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument proving that the essence (dhāt) of Allah is unknown to humans. The author (Sanusi) begins by stating that when it is established that the most specific attribute (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of the Creator is unknown, it follows that His essence is unknown to humans. He declares this the sounder view (al-aṣaḥḥ) and names its proponents: al-Qāḍī (Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī), Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), Ḥujjat al-Islām (al-Ghazālī), and Imām al-Fakhr (Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī). He notes an exception: al-Rāzī in his early work 'al-Ishāra' held the opposite, that the essence is known. The author then raises a subsidiary question: is the denial of knowledge absolute (including in the Hereafter) or only in this life? He reports that al-Imām (al-Rāzī?) and al-Ghazālī deny absolutely, while al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār withhold judgment. Next, he presents and refutes the arguments of those who claim the essence is known. Their first argument is from Moses' answer to Pharaoh in the Qur'an, but the author says it has already been refuted. Their second argument is that we make judgments about Allah (e.g., He is existent, powerful), and judgment presupposes knowledge. The refutation is that judgment only requires some awareness (shuʿūr) of the thing, even a general external awareness, not knowledge of the essence itself, which is the point in dispute. Then the author presents the proofs of the correct view, divided into transmitted (manqūl) and rational (maʿqūl). The transmitted proofs are two Qur'anic verses: 'they encompass Him not in knowledge' (Ṭā Hā 110) and 'Eyesight comprehends Him not' (al-Anʿām 103) with a qualification. Additionally, a verbal hint: the name 'Allāh' is derived from the bewilderment (walāh) of intellects. The author adds that the incapacity of intellects to grasp Allah's majesty is almost known from religion by necessity. The rational proof is from Imam Fakhr al-Dīn: human knowledge comprises only four categories: existence, modes of existence (eternity, perpetuity, necessity), negations (not a body, etc.), and relative attributes (knowing, powerful). The specific essence that is described by these concepts is distinct from them, and we have no awareness of that essence except that it is described by these attributes. Therefore, the specific essence is unknown. This argument shows that all we know are attributes, not the essence itself.

Key Terms
الذات
الأخص
المعلوم
الأصح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) That knowledge is possible only in the Hereafter

Key Takeaway
The sounder view among theologians is that Allah's essence is unknown to humans, supported by major scholars and with nuances about its absoluteness.

?
You have learned that even the greatest scholars admitted they cannot know Allah's essence. Where in your life do you act as if you have fully grasped Him? What would it look like to live with the constant awareness that Allah is beyond your comprehension?

Refutation of Arguments for Knowing the Essence
Refutation of two opposing arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments used by those who claim Allah's essence is known and how Sanusi refutes them.

Core Idea
Judging about Allah requires only awareness, not essence knowledge.

Sanusi presents and refutes two arguments from those who say the essence is known: the story of Moses and Pharaoh, and the claim that judgment presupposes knowledge of essence. Both are shown to be invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument and its refutation
The first argument is from Moses' answer to Pharaoh. Sanusi says it has already been refuted. Note that the refutation is not repeated here.
2
Read the second argument
The second argument: we make judgments about Allah (e.g., He is existent, powerful), and judgment about a thing requires knowledge of it.
This seems plausible at first.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi refutes by distinguishing: judgment requires awareness (shuʿūr) of the thing in some way, not knowledge of the essence itself. The awareness of a general external aspect is sufficient.
This is a key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : وإذا تبيَّنَ لكَ أنَّ أخصَّ وصْفِ الباري جلَّ وعلا مجهول . . عرفتَ أنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى غيرُ معروفة للبشرِ ، وهو الأصحُ مِنَ القولين وإليه ذهب القاضي وإمام الحرمين وحُجَّةُ الإسلام والإمام الفخرُ في أكثرِ كتبِهِ ، واختارَ في كتاب ( الإشارة ) ـ وهو مِنْ أَوَّلِ مُصنَّفَاتِهِ - أنَّها معلومة ، وعلى المنع : فهل هو مطلقاً ولو في الآخرة ، أو إنَّما في الحال ، ويجوز أن تصير معلومة بعد ؟ نقل سيفُ الدينِ عنِ الإمامِ والغزالي المنع مطلقاً ، ونقل فيهِ الوقف عن القاضي وضرار واحتجَّ مَنْ قالَ بأنَّ حقيقةَ الذاتِ الكريمة معلومة : بما تقدَّمَ مِنْ جواب موسى عليه السلام لفرعون حين سألَهُ عن الحقيقة ، وقد سبق ردُّه ، واحتجوا أيضاً : بأنَّا نحكم على الذات العليَّةِ بأحكام ، والحكم على الشيء فرعُ معرفتِهِ ، وهو مردودٌ بأنَّ الحكم على الشيء فرع الشعور به بوجه ما ، ولو بوجه خارجي إجمالي ، لا فرع معرفةِ ذاتِهِ التي هي محل النزاع واحتج القائلون بأنَّها غير معلومة بالمنقول والمعقول : أما المنقولُ : فقوله تعالى : ( وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه : ١١٠] ، وقوله : ( لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام : (۱۰۳) على وجه ، وقد قيل : إنَّما سُمِّيَ : ( الله ) مِنْ وَلَهِ العقولِ وتحيرها في كُنْهِ جلالِهِ تعالى . وبالجملة : فعجْزُ العقولِ عنِ الإحاطة بعظيم كبريائه جلَّ وعلا وباهر جماله وعلي جلالِهِ ، بل عجزها عن عجائب صُنْعِهِ في مخلوقاته .. يكاد أن يكونَ معلوماً مِنَ الدين ضرورةً . وأما المعقولُ : فقال الإمام فخر الدين : ( الدليل عليهِ : أَنَّ المعلوم عندَ البشرِ أمورٌ أربعةُ : إمَّا الوجود ، وإمَّا كيفيات الوجودِ ؛ الأزليَّة والأبدية والوجوب ، وإمَّا السُّلوبُ ؛ وإمَّا السُّلوبُ ؛ وهي أَنَّهُ ليسَ بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ، وإمَّا الإضافية ؛ وهي العالميَّةُ والقادرية ، والذات المخصوصة الموصوفة بهذه المفهوماتِ مغايرة لها لا محالة ، وليسَ عندَنا مِنْ تلكَ الذاتِ المخصوصة إلا أنَّها ذاتُ لا ندري ما هي إلا أنَّها موصوفةٌ بهذهِ الصفاتِ ، وهذا يدلُّ على أنَّ ذاته المخصوصة غير معلومة
I say: When it has become clear to you that the most specific attribute of the Creator, majestic and exalted, is unknown... you know that His essence, exalted is He, is unknown to humans, and this is the sounder of the two views. To it went al-Qāḍī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn, Ḥujjat al-Islām, and Imām al-Fakhr in most of his books. He chose in the book 'al-Ishāra' – which is among his earliest works – that it is known. Regarding the denial: is it absolute, even in the Hereafter, or only in the present state, and it may become known afterwards? Sayf al-Dīn narrated from al-Imām and al-Ghazālī the absolute denial, and he narrated from al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār that they stopped. And those who said that the reality of the noble essence is known argued with the previously mentioned answer of Moses, peace be upon him, to Pharaoh when he asked about the reality, and its refutation has already preceded. They also argued that we make judgments about the supreme essence, and judgment about a thing is a branch of knowledge of it. This is refuted because judgment about a thing is a branch of awareness of it in some way, even if by a general external aspect, not a branch of knowledge of its essence, which is the point of contention. And those who say it is unknown argued by transmitted and rational [proofs]. As for the transmitted: His saying, exalted: 'and they encompass Him not in knowledge' [Ṭā Hā 110], and His saying: 'Eyesight comprehends Him not' [al-Anʿām 103] in one sense. And it has been said: He was named 'Allāh' only from the bewilderment of intellects and their perplexity at the reality of His majesty, exalted. And in summary: the incapacity of intellects to encompass His great majesty, glorious and exalted, and His dazzling beauty and lofty sublimity, rather their incapacity regarding the wonders of His handiwork in His creation... is almost known from religion by necessity. As for the rational [proof], Imām Fakhr al-Dīn said: 'The evidence for it is that what is known to humans is four things: either existence, or modes of existence: eternity, perpetuity, and necessity; or negations: that He is not a body, not a substance, not an accident; or relative attributes: being all-knowing and all-powerful. And the specific essence described by these concepts is necessarily distinct from them. And we have nothing of that specific essence except that it is an essence we do not know what it is except that it is described by these attributes. And this indicates that His specific essence is unknown.'
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument proving that the essence (dhāt) of Allah is unknown to humans. The author (Sanusi) begins by stating that when it is established that the most specific attribute (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of the Creator is unknown, it follows that His essence is unknown to humans. He declares this the sounder view (al-aṣaḥḥ) and names its proponents: al-Qāḍī (Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī), Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), Ḥujjat al-Islām (al-Ghazālī), and Imām al-Fakhr (Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī). He notes an exception: al-Rāzī in his early work 'al-Ishāra' held the opposite, that the essence is known. The author then raises a subsidiary question: is the denial of knowledge absolute (including in the Hereafter) or only in this life? He reports that al-Imām (al-Rāzī?) and al-Ghazālī deny absolutely, while al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār withhold judgment. Next, he presents and refutes the arguments of those who claim the essence is known. Their first argument is from Moses' answer to Pharaoh in the Qur'an, but the author says it has already been refuted. Their second argument is that we make judgments about Allah (e.g., He is existent, powerful), and judgment presupposes knowledge. The refutation is that judgment only requires some awareness (shuʿūr) of the thing, even a general external awareness, not knowledge of the essence itself, which is the point in dispute. Then the author presents the proofs of the correct view, divided into transmitted (manqūl) and rational (maʿqūl). The transmitted proofs are two Qur'anic verses: 'they encompass Him not in knowledge' (Ṭā Hā 110) and 'Eyesight comprehends Him not' (al-Anʿām 103) with a qualification. Additionally, a verbal hint: the name 'Allāh' is derived from the bewilderment (walāh) of intellects. The author adds that the incapacity of intellects to grasp Allah's majesty is almost known from religion by necessity. The rational proof is from Imam Fakhr al-Dīn: human knowledge comprises only four categories: existence, modes of existence (eternity, perpetuity, necessity), negations (not a body, etc.), and relative attributes (knowing, powerful). The specific essence that is described by these concepts is distinct from them, and we have no awareness of that essence except that it is described by these attributes. Therefore, the specific essence is unknown. This argument shows that all we know are attributes, not the essence itself.

Key Terms
المعلوم
الذات
الشعور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if we can describe Allah, we know His essence.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that we only have external general awareness, not inner knowledge of the essence.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi refute the argument that judgment about Allah requires knowledge of His essence?
A) He says judgment requires faith, not knowledge
B) He distinguishes between awareness and essence knowledge
C) He claims the argument is invalid because of logical fallacy
D) He says the essence is knowable through attributes

Key Takeaway
Judgments about Allah are based on general awareness of His attributes, not on knowledge of His unknowable essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's attributes like 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful', do you ever unconsciously assume you have grasped His very essence? How would it change your prayers and humility if you truly internalized that you are only aware of Him through a 'general external aspect'? Write one specific way you will remind yourself of this boundary in your next conversation about Allah.
?
Has there been a time when you demanded to 'understand the essence' of a divine command before obeying it, thinking that full knowledge must come first? How does this lesson's separation of awareness from essence knowledge free you to act on what you do know—His attributes—rather than waiting for complete comprehension? Commit to one area where you will submit based on awareness, not full understanding.
?
The opponents in this passage used rational argument to claim they knew Allah's essence. Have you ever felt pride in your theological knowledge, as if you have 'figured out' Allah? How can this lesson's refutation keep you humble and grounded, knowing that even your most precise judgments are only based on general awareness? What specific habit can you adopt to remind yourself of your limited knowledge of the Divine essence?

Rational Judgments

Transmitted Proofs That Allah's Essence Is Unknown
Transmitted proofs from Qur'an and language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the two Qur'anic verses and the linguistic derivation used as transmitted proofs that Allah's essence is unknown.

Core Idea
The Qur'an and derivation of Allah's name prove essence unknowability.

Sanusi presents the transmitted (manqūl) proofs for the doctrine: two Qur'anic verses and the etymology of the name 'Allāh' from bewilderment. He also notes that the incapacity of intellects is virtually known by necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the first transmitted proof
The verse 'They encompass Him not in knowledge' (Ṭā Hā 110) directly states that creation cannot fully encompass Allah in knowledge.
2
Read the second verse
The verse 'Eyesight comprehends Him not' (al-Anʿām 103) with a qualification ('on one sense') indicates that even vision cannot grasp Him, implying essence is beyond perception.
3
Examine the linguistic proof
The name 'Allāh' is said to be derived from walāh (bewilderment), because intellects are bewildered by His majesty. This supports the idea that His essence is unfathomable.
This is a subtle but powerful point.
4
Reflect on the concluding statement
Sanusi says the incapacity of intellects to grasp His majesty is almost known from religion by necessity, emphasizing the certainty of the doctrine.

Which Qur'anic verse is cited as transmitted proof that Allah's essence is unknown?
A) 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (al-Nūr 35)
B) 'And they encompass Him not in knowledge' (Ṭā Hā 110)

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that your intellect cannot grasp Allah's essence, or do you secretly feel you understand Him enough to judge what He should do? How does this affect your patience with divine decrees you don't understand?
?
When you say 'Allah' do you remember that this name itself comes from the bewilderment of intellects? How can this humility reshape the way you approach your prayers today?
?
If the incapacity to encompass Allah's essence is 'almost known from religion by necessity', why do you still find yourself trying to define Him in your mind or feeling dissatisfied with not knowing? What would change if you embraced this limitation completely?

Rational Proof: Knowledge Limited to Four Categories
Rational proof from Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that human knowledge is limited to four categories and that the essence of Allah is distinct from them, therefore unknown.

Core Idea
All we know are attributes; the essence itself remains unknown.

Sanusi presents Imam Fakhr al-Dīn's rational argument: everything known to humans falls into four categories – existence, modes, negations, and relations. The specific essence described by these is distinct and not known in itself. This proves the essence is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Read the four categories of knowledge
Fakhr al-Dīn says human knowledge is limited to: existence, modes of existence (eternity, perpetuity, necessity), negations (not a body, etc.), and relative attributes (knowing, powerful).
These are the only things we can know about anything.
2
Understand the argument's core
The specific essence described by these categories is distinct from them. We have no direct knowledge of that essence except that it is described by these attributes.
This is the key point.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since we only know the attributes and not the essence itself, the specific essence remains unknown.

قلتُ : وإذا تبيَّنَ لكَ أنَّ أخصَّ وصْفِ الباري جلَّ وعلا مجهول . . عرفتَ أنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى غيرُ معروفة للبشرِ ، وهو الأصحُ مِنَ القولين وإليه ذهب القاضي وإمام الحرمين وحُجَّةُ الإسلام والإمام الفخرُ في أكثرِ كتبِهِ ، واختارَ في كتاب ( الإشارة ) ـ وهو مِنْ أَوَّلِ مُصنَّفَاتِهِ - أنَّها معلومة ، وعلى المنع : فهل هو مطلقاً ولو في الآخرة ، أو إنَّما في الحال ، ويجوز أن تصير معلومة بعد ؟ نقل سيفُ الدينِ عنِ الإمامِ والغزالي المنع مطلقاً ، ونقل فيهِ الوقف عن القاضي وضرار واحتجَّ مَنْ قالَ بأنَّ حقيقةَ الذاتِ الكريمة معلومة : بما تقدَّمَ مِنْ جواب موسى عليه السلام لفرعون حين سألَهُ عن الحقيقة ، وقد سبق ردُّه ، واحتجوا أيضاً : بأنَّا نحكم على الذات العليَّةِ بأحكام ، والحكم على الشيء فرعُ معرفتِهِ ، وهو مردودٌ بأنَّ الحكم على الشيء فرع الشعور به بوجه ما ، ولو بوجه خارجي إجمالي ، لا فرع معرفةِ ذاتِهِ التي هي محل النزاع واحتج القائلون بأنَّها غير معلومة بالمنقول والمعقول : أما المنقولُ : فقوله تعالى : ( وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه : ١١٠] ، وقوله : ( لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام : (۱۰۳) على وجه ، وقد قيل : إنَّما سُمِّيَ : ( الله ) مِنْ وَلَهِ العقولِ وتحيرها في كُنْهِ جلالِهِ تعالى . وبالجملة : فعجْزُ العقولِ عنِ الإحاطة بعظيم كبريائه جلَّ وعلا وباهر جماله وعلي جلالِهِ ، بل عجزها عن عجائب صُنْعِهِ في مخلوقاته .. يكاد أن يكونَ معلوماً مِنَ الدين ضرورةً . وأما المعقولُ : فقال الإمام فخر الدين : ( الدليل عليهِ : أَنَّ المعلوم عندَ البشرِ أمورٌ أربعةُ : إمَّا الوجود ، وإمَّا كيفيات الوجودِ ؛ الأزليَّة والأبدية والوجوب ، وإمَّا السُّلوبُ ؛ وإمَّا السُّلوبُ ؛ وهي أَنَّهُ ليسَ بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ، وإمَّا الإضافية ؛ وهي العالميَّةُ والقادرية ، والذات المخصوصة الموصوفة بهذه المفهوماتِ مغايرة لها لا محالة ، وليسَ عندَنا مِنْ تلكَ الذاتِ المخصوصة إلا أنَّها ذاتُ لا ندري ما هي إلا أنَّها موصوفةٌ بهذهِ الصفاتِ ، وهذا يدلُّ على أنَّ ذاته المخصوصة غير معلومة
I say: When it has become clear to you that the most specific attribute of the Creator, majestic and exalted, is unknown... you know that His essence, exalted is He, is unknown to humans, and this is the sounder of the two views. To it went al-Qāḍī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn, Ḥujjat al-Islām, and Imām al-Fakhr in most of his books. He chose in the book 'al-Ishāra' – which is among his earliest works – that it is known. Regarding the denial: is it absolute, even in the Hereafter, or only in the present state, and it may become known afterwards? Sayf al-Dīn narrated from al-Imām and al-Ghazālī the absolute denial, and he narrated from al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār that they stopped. And those who said that the reality of the noble essence is known argued with the previously mentioned answer of Moses, peace be upon him, to Pharaoh when he asked about the reality, and its refutation has already preceded. They also argued that we make judgments about the supreme essence, and judgment about a thing is a branch of knowledge of it. This is refuted because judgment about a thing is a branch of awareness of it in some way, even if by a general external aspect, not a branch of knowledge of its essence, which is the point of contention. And those who say it is unknown argued by transmitted and rational [proofs]. As for the transmitted: His saying, exalted: 'and they encompass Him not in knowledge' [Ṭā Hā 110], and His saying: 'Eyesight comprehends Him not' [al-Anʿām 103] in one sense. And it has been said: He was named 'Allāh' only from the bewilderment of intellects and their perplexity at the reality of His majesty, exalted. And in summary: the incapacity of intellects to encompass His great majesty, glorious and exalted, and His dazzling beauty and lofty sublimity, rather their incapacity regarding the wonders of His handiwork in His creation... is almost known from religion by necessity. As for the rational [proof], Imām Fakhr al-Dīn said: 'The evidence for it is that what is known to humans is four things: either existence, or modes of existence: eternity, perpetuity, and necessity; or negations: that He is not a body, not a substance, not an accident; or relative attributes: being all-knowing and all-powerful. And the specific essence described by these concepts is necessarily distinct from them. And we have nothing of that specific essence except that it is an essence we do not know what it is except that it is described by these attributes. And this indicates that His specific essence is unknown.'
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument proving that the essence (dhāt) of Allah is unknown to humans. The author (Sanusi) begins by stating that when it is established that the most specific attribute (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of the Creator is unknown, it follows that His essence is unknown to humans. He declares this the sounder view (al-aṣaḥḥ) and names its proponents: al-Qāḍī (Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī), Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), Ḥujjat al-Islām (al-Ghazālī), and Imām al-Fakhr (Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī). He notes an exception: al-Rāzī in his early work 'al-Ishāra' held the opposite, that the essence is known. The author then raises a subsidiary question: is the denial of knowledge absolute (including in the Hereafter) or only in this life? He reports that al-Imām (al-Rāzī?) and al-Ghazālī deny absolutely, while al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār withhold judgment. Next, he presents and refutes the arguments of those who claim the essence is known. Their first argument is from Moses' answer to Pharaoh in the Qur'an, but the author says it has already been refuted. Their second argument is that we make judgments about Allah (e.g., He is existent, powerful), and judgment presupposes knowledge. The refutation is that judgment only requires some awareness (shuʿūr) of the thing, even a general external awareness, not knowledge of the essence itself, which is the point in dispute. Then the author presents the proofs of the correct view, divided into transmitted (manqūl) and rational (maʿqūl). The transmitted proofs are two Qur'anic verses: 'they encompass Him not in knowledge' (Ṭā Hā 110) and 'Eyesight comprehends Him not' (al-Anʿām 103) with a qualification. Additionally, a verbal hint: the name 'Allāh' is derived from the bewilderment (walāh) of intellects. The author adds that the incapacity of intellects to grasp Allah's majesty is almost known from religion by necessity. The rational proof is from Imam Fakhr al-Dīn: human knowledge comprises only four categories: existence, modes of existence (eternity, perpetuity, necessity), negations (not a body, etc.), and relative attributes (knowing, powerful). The specific essence that is described by these concepts is distinct from them, and we have no awareness of that essence except that it is described by these attributes. Therefore, the specific essence is unknown. This argument shows that all we know are attributes, not the essence itself.

Key Terms
المعقول
الوجود
الكيفيات
السُّلوب
الإضافية
الذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that listing attributes gives comprehensive knowledge of Allah's essence.
Correction: The attributes only describe aspects; the essence itself remains unknown.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Human knowledge is limited to attributes; the specific essence of Allah is distinct and unknown, proving the doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you find yourself content with knowing only His attributes, or does your heart secretly desire to grasp His essence? How would it change your prayer tonight to fully admit that His essence is beyond your knowing?
?
This proof reduces everything you can know about Allah to just four categories. Does that limitation feel humbling or frustrating to you? What would it mean for your worship if you truly embraced that you will never 'figure out' who Allah is in His essence?
?
If the specific essence of Allah is completely unknown, then every time you speak of Allah, you are speaking about attributes, not the essence. Are the words you use in your daily life about Allah spoken with this humility, or do you speak as if you have grasped Him? Commit to one change in your speech about Allah after reading this.

The Meaning of Wujub and Its Defense
Author defines wujub and refutes a proof that it is a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that necessity (wujub) is not a positive attribute but a negation of non-existence, and how to refute the counterargument that it is a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Necessity is the negation of non-existence, not a positive attribute.

Before we can discuss God's attributes, we must clarify what 'necessary' means. Is it a positive quality or a mere negation? The author defines wujub and then answers an objection that it is a positive attribute. This lesson unpacks that definition and the logic of the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get a sense of the argument without analysis.
Notice the flow: definition, objection, response.
2
Understand the definition of wujub
The author defines wujub as 'negation of subsequent non-existence' – a negative concept, not positive.
3
Identify the objection
Someone argues that wujub is thubut (positive attribute) because it affirms existence.
The reasoning is that affirmation is realization, and a thing cannot be realized by its opposite.
4
Follow the author's response
The author replies that affirmation can be achieved by negating the opposite, using the example 'this is true, no doubt about it'.
Thus necessity is not a separate positive attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلبُ العدمِ اللاحقِ ، ومعنى الوجوبِ : أَنَّهُ لا يقبلُ الانتفاء بحال ومَنِ احتج على أنَّهُ ثبوت ؛ بأنَّهُ يُؤكِّدُ بهِ الوجود ، وتأكيد الشيء تحقيقه ، والشيء لا يُحقَّقُ بنقيضه.. فجوابُهُ : أَنَّهُ يُحقَّقُ بسلْبٍ نقيضه ؛ كقولنا : هذا حق لا شك فيه ، كذا نقولُ : وجود واجبٌ ؛ أي : لا ينتفي بحالٍ
It is the negation of subsequent non-existence, and the meaning of necessity (wujub): that it does not admit of non-existence at all. And whoever argued that it is a positive attribute (thubut) because it affirms existence, and affirmation of a thing is its realization, and a thing is not realized by its opposite... the answer: it is realized by negating its opposite, as we say: 'This is true, no doubt about it,' likewise we say: 'Existence is necessary,' meaning: it does not cease to exist at all.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'wajib' (necessary) in the context of rational judgments. The author states that necessity is essentially a negation: it is the negation of subsequent non-existence, meaning that a necessary being cannot possibly not exist. He then addresses an objection: someone argued that necessity is a positive attribute (thubut) because it affirms existence. Their reasoning is that affirming something is realizing it, and a thing cannot be realized by its opposite (i.e., negation). The author responds that in fact, affirmation is achieved by negating the opposite, not by adding a positive reality. He gives the example: 'This is true, no doubt about it.' The phrase 'no doubt' is a negation that confirms the truth. Similarly, calling existence 'necessary' means it never ceases to exist. Thus necessity is not a positive attribute but a negation of non-existence.

Key Terms
الوجوب (wujub)
الثبوت (thubut)
سلب (salb)
العدم (adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity (wujub) is a positive attribute (thubut) added to existence.
Correction: Necessity is actually the negation of non-existence; it does not add a positive quality but denies the possibility of non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author refute the claim that necessity (wujub) is a positive attribute (thubut)?
A) By showing that necessity is a relational attribute
B) By arguing that affirmation is achieved through negation of the opposite
C) By stating that necessity is the same as existence
D) By denying that necessity applies to God

Key Takeaway
Necessity (wujub) is the negation of non-existence, not a positive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is necessary,' do you truly feel that He cannot cease, or do your actions show a hidden fear that He might? How would your life change if you internalized that necessity is just the negation of non-existence—a simple but profound denial of any possibility of His absence?
?
Think of a moment when you felt distant from Allah. Did you treat His nearness as a positive attribute that could be lost, rather than as a necessary reality that never departs? How can the lesson that affirmation comes through negation—like affirming existence by negating non-existence—rebuild your certainty that He is always there, even when you don't feel Him?

The Possibility of Direct Knowledge of God
Sanusi defends the possibility of direct mystical knowledge granted by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the possibility of direct mystical knowledge while rejecting extreme claims, and the rational argument he employs.

Core Idea
Direct knowledge from God is possible, but not equal to prophethood.

Sanusi acknowledges that God can grant special knowledge to His servants, as He did to al-Khiḍr, but he distinguishes this from claims of immediate vision or sharing in prophethood. He then uses an argument from the possibility of perceiving God in the afterlife to argue that the intellect cannot deem impossible a similar perception in this life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's tone and structure.
The passage begins with a quotation and then makes a distinction.
2
Identify the acceptable and unacceptable claims
Find where the author states what he does not deny (knowledge granted by God) and what he explicitly denies (immediate vision, advancement to prophethood, etc.).
3
Trace the argument from afterlife perception
The author argues that since God can create a more perfect perception of Himself in the afterlife, it is not impossible for Him to create a similar perception in the heart in this life. Understand the analogy.
This is the core rational argument.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion and humility
The author admits his own limitation in knowing other spiritual states and concludes that such knowledge is possible and not impossible, citing the Quranic command to ask for more knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُقالُ : لن يفهم عنكَ إلا مَنْ أشرقَ فيه ما أشرق فيكَ ، ولا يعنون بذلك حلولاً كما يفهمُهُ بعضُ المُلبّسينَ ، بل يريدون تلك البصيرة الباطنة والموهبة الربانية التي لا ريب فيها ولا شك ، كما وصف بذلك نبيَّة عليهِ السلامُ فقالَ : ﴿ مَا زَاغَ الْبَصَرُ وَمَا طَغَى ﴾ [ النجم : ۱۷] ، فأَنَّى لهُ الجزم بنفي جميع ما يدعونه ؟! ونحن لا ننكرُ أنْ يخصّ الله تعالى عبداً مِنْ عبيده بعلم ما ، كما قالَ تعالى في الخضرِ : ﴿ وَعَلَّمْنَهُ مِن لَّدُنَا عِلْمًا ﴾ [الكهف : ٦٥] ، وإِنَّما ننكرُ على مَنْ يدَّعي رؤيةً عاجلةً ، أو تقدُّماً على درجة النبوَّةِ ، أو مشاركةً فيها ، أو أنَّهُ عالم بالله تعالى علم إحاطة . وإذا جازَ خَلْقُ إدراك لنا بالله تعالى في الآخرة هو أتم إدراكاً مِنْ إدراكنا الذي هو معرفة المؤثر بأثره .. فلا يجزم العقلُ باستحالة خلق مثل ذلك في القلبِ ، ويكون نسبةً ما تعلّق به في الوضوح والجلاء كنسبة الحاصل عن الرؤية . فالحقُ إذاً : أنْ نجزم بجواز ذلكَ ، ولا استحالة ، وإذا كانَ ذلك يرجعُ إلى الوجدانِ - وفضلُ اللهِ تعالى لا نهاية له ـ فلا علم لي إلا بحالِ نفسي ، وحال غيري لا أعرفُهُ إلا بإنباء صادق في العادة ، ولم يُوجَدْ ، وما يدعيه الصوفية لم نميزه فنعلم أنَّ ذلكَ المُدرَكَ يرجع إلى الذاتِ مِنْ وجه أو إلى ترقُ في العلم بالصفاتِ والأسماء .. فكيف لنا بالجزمِ أنَّ الله تعالى لم يخلق لصديق ولا نبي مرسل سوى ما نعلمه نحنُ مِنَ الله تعالى ؟! والله عزَّ وجلَّ يقول لأعلم الخلْقِ : ﴿ وَقُل رَّبِّ زِدْنِي عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه : ١١٤] ، ومُتعلّق السؤال المأمور به ممكن ، والله أعلم .
And it is said: None will understand from you except one in whom there shined what shined in you. They do not mean by that incarnation (ḥulūl) as some of the confused ones understand, but they mean that inner insight and divine gift which has no doubt or uncertainty, just as the Prophet, peace be upon him, described it when he said: 'His sight did not swerve, nor did it transgress' (Quran 53:17). So how can he be certain to deny all that they claim? And we do not deny that Allah the Exalted may single out a servant from His servants with some knowledge, as He said concerning al-Khiḍr: 'And We taught him from Our presence a knowledge' (Quran 18:65). Rather, we deny only the one who claims an immediate vision, or advancement to the rank of prophethood, or sharing in it, or that he knows Allah the Exalted with encompassing knowledge. And if it is possible for Allah to create for us a perception of Him in the afterlife that is more complete than our perception which is knowing the effect through its cause, then the intellect cannot decisively judge the impossibility of creating something similar in the heart. And the clarity and obviousness of what is perceived would be in the same proportion as what results from vision. So the truth is that we must be certain of its permissibility, and there is no impossibility. And if that pertains to inner experience (wijdān) — and the bounty of Allah has no end — then I have no knowledge except of my own state, and the state of another I do not know except by truthful report in the usual course of things, and such a report has not existed. And what the Sufis claim we have not distinguished so as to know whether that perceived thing pertains to the Essence in some respect or to an advancement in knowledge of the attributes and names. So how can we be certain that Allah the Exalted has not created for any friend or sent prophet other than what we ourselves know of Allah?! And Allah, Mighty and Majestic, says to the most knowledgeable of creation: 'And say: My Lord, increase me in knowledge' (Quran 20:114), and the object of the commanded request is possible, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the possibility of direct mystical knowledge (dhawq, kashf) against those who deny it outright. The author begins by quoting a saying: 'None will understand from you except one in whom there shined what shined in you,' clarifying that this does not imply incarnation (ḥulūl) as some misguided people think, but rather refers to an inner insight and divine gift that is certain. He supports this by referencing the Prophet's vision during the Miʿrāj (Quran 53:17), arguing that if the Prophet's sight did not err, how can one categorically deny all such claims? The author then distinguishes between what is acceptable and what is not: he does not deny that God can grant special knowledge to a servant, as He did to al-Khiḍr (Quran 18:65). What he denies is the claim of an immediate vision (ruʾya ʿājila), or equality with or sharing in prophethood, or encompassing knowledge (ʿilm iḥāṭa) of God. The core argument follows: since God can create for us a perception of Himself in the afterlife that is more perfect than our current knowledge (which is inference from effects to the Cause), then the intellect cannot deem impossible the creation of a similar perception in this life in the heart. The clarity of such perception would be analogous to that of vision. Therefore, the truth is to affirm its possibility (jawāz) and deny its impossibility. The author then humbly states that he has no knowledge of others' spiritual states except by reliable report, and no such report has come. Since we cannot even distinguish whether what the Sufis claim pertains to the Essence or only to attributes and names, how can we be certain that God has not granted such knowledge to some friends or prophets? He concludes by citing the Quranic verse commanding the Prophet to ask for more knowledge (Quran 20:114), implying that the object of that request is possible, and God knows best.

Key Terms
ذوق
حلول
بصيرة باطنة
موهبة ربانية
علم إحاطة
وجدان
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sanusi denies all forms of mystical knowledge.
Correction: He only denies claims of immediate vision or equality with prophethood, not the possibility of special knowledge from God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which claim does he explicitly reject?
A) That God can grant special knowledge to a servant.
B) That a person can have an immediate vision or advancement to the rank of prophethood.
C) That the Prophet's sight did not swerve during the Miʿrāj.
D) That the afterlife perception of God is more complete than current knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi affirms the possibility of direct knowledge from God as a divine gift, while rejecting claims that equate it to prophethood or imply incarnation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe you know God through His effects. But if God grants a direct perception to whom He wills, do you truly desire that gift, or are you content with your current knowledge? Are you afraid of being tested by such a gift? What would you ask for if you were commanded to seek increase in knowledge, like the Prophet? This lesson shows that such knowledge is possible. Examine your heart: are you open to the possibility that God might grant you a deeper insight, or do you dismiss it because it seems too far from your experience? How would your life change if you sincerely believed God could grant you direct knowledge?
?
The author states that he does not know the state of others except by reliable report, and no such report has come. How often do you judge others' spiritual claims without evidence? When you hear of someone claiming a spiritual experience, do you immediately deny it, or do you reserve judgment as Sanusi does? What does your reaction reveal about your own humility before God's limitless bounty?
?
The author says the clarity of such perception would be like that of vision. Imagine you had such a clear perception of God. Would your priorities shift? Would your sins seem more grievous? This lesson affirms that such knowledge is possible. If you truly believed that, what would you change about your daily worship? Are you seeking knowledge from God, or are you satisfied with merely academic learning?

Refuting Fakhr al-Razi's Epistemological Argument
Sanusi presents and refutes Fakhr al-Razi's epistemological proof regarding knowledge of God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes Fakhr al-Razi's claim that God's essence is unknowable by challenging the restricted epistemology and affirming God's power to grant direct necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
God can grant direct necessary knowledge of His essence to whom He wills.

Fakhr al-Razi argued that we can only conceive what we perceive through senses, inner experience, or intellectual intuition—and God's essence is none of these. Sanusi refutes this by denying the restriction and affirming God's power to create necessary knowledge. Let's read his argument and see how he defends the knowability of God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read Fakhr's Argument
Read the passage carefully, noting the three sources Fakhr lists: senses (ḥiss), inner experience (wijdān), and intellectual intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql). Understand his conclusion that God's essence is not perceived.
Fakhr's argument is a typical philosophical proof based on empiricism and rationalism.
2
Identify Sanusi's First Objection
Sanusi begins his refutation by denying the restriction (ḥaṣr) of the sources. Find the phrase 'The objection: denial of the restriction' and note why he says it is invalid.
The restriction is based on Fakhr's own view that all concepts are non-acquired, which is not accepted by all.
3
Understand the Conditional Argument
Even if we concede that necessary knowledge is a source, Sanusi argues that God can create it for some servants about His essence. This bypasses Fakhr's limitation.
This argument relies on Allah's absolute power and bounty.
4
Reflect on the Theological Implications
Consider the broader meaning: knowledge of God's essence is possible through divine grace, not limited to human faculties. This affirms God's generosity.
Sanusi ends by quoting the Quran: 'And the bounty of Allah He gives to whom He wills.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الفخر أيضاً : بأنَّا لا نتصوَّرُ إلا ما أدركناه بالحس ، ومثاله معلوم ، أو بالوجدان ، كالألم واللذَّةِ ، أو ببديهة العقل ؛ كبسائط القضايا الأوَّليَّةِ ، وهي كقولنا : النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان ، فهذهِ طُرُقُ معرفةِ التصوراتِ ، وماهية الباري تعالى غيرُ مُدرَكة بالحس ولا بالوجدانِ ولا ببديهة العقل ، فليسَتْ مُدرَكةً لنا . والاعتراض : منع حصر مداركِ التصوراتِ فيما ذَكَرَ ؛ لأنه بناءً على رأيه في التصوراتِ أنها كلها غير مكتسبة ، وهو ممنوع . ثم إذا سُلَّمَ أنَّ مِنْ طرقها العلم الضروري.. فأي مانعِ مِنْ أن يخلق الله تعالى لبعض عبيدِهِ علماً ضرورياً بأمرٍ ما لم تجرِ العادة بخلْقِ العلم بمثلِهِ ؟! وفضلُ اللَّهِ يؤتِيهِ مَنْ يَشَاءُ يشاء ، والله ذو الفضل العظيم
And Fakhr [al-Rāzī] also argued: that we do not conceive except what we have perceived by the senses, and its like is known; or by inner experience (wijdān), such as pain and pleasure; or by intellectual intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql), such as the simple primary propositions, like our saying: negation and affirmation do not coexist nor do they both cease. So these are the means of knowing concepts (taṣawwurāt), and the essence (māhiyya) of the Creator, Exalted, is not perceived by the senses, nor by inner experience, nor by intellectual intuition, so it is not perceived by us. The objection: denial of the restriction of the means of concepts to what he mentioned; because it is based on his view that concepts are all non-acquired (ghayr muktasaba), and this is denied. Then if it is conceded that from among its means is necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī)... so what prevents Allah the Exalted from creating for some of His servants necessary knowledge of something for which the usual course does not create such knowledge?! And the bounty of Allah He gives to whom He wills, and Allah is the possessor of great bounty.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from Fakhr al-Rāzī and the author's refutation of it. The passage is an objection-and-answer unit. First, Fakhr's argument is stated: our only means of acquiring concepts (taṣawwurāt) are three: sense perception, inner experience (like pain and pleasure), and intellectual intuition (primary propositions). God's essence (māhiyya) is not accessible through any of these, so we cannot conceive it. The author's response begins by denying the restriction (ḥaṣr) of the means to just these three. He points out that Fakhr's enumeration is based on his own view that all concepts are non-acquired (ghayr muktasaba) — i.e., necessarily known — and this view is itself denied. Then the author makes a conditional argument: even if we accept that one of the means is necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī), what prevents God from creating necessary knowledge in some of His servants about something not usually known by that method? In other words, God can grant direct, necessary knowledge of His essence to whomever He wills. The author concludes by affirming God's boundless bounty: He gives His bounty to whom He wills, and He is the possessor of great bounty.

إدراك
حس
وجدان
بديهة العقل
ماهية
علم ضروري
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Sanusi agrees with Fakhr that God's essence is inconceivable.
Correction: Sanusi does not agree; he refutes Fakhr's argument by denying the restriction of sources and affirming that God can grant direct necessary knowledge of His essence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main objection to Fakhr al-Razi's argument?
A) He agrees that we cannot conceive God's essence.
B) He denies that the means of conceiving are limited to senses, inner experience, and intellectual intuition.
C) He argues that God's essence can be perceived by the senses.
D) He claims that inner experience is not a valid source of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes Fakhr al-Razi by denying the limited sources of knowledge and asserting that God can grant direct, necessary knowledge of His essence to whom He wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can grant direct necessary knowledge of His essence to whomever He wills, what is your relationship with that knowledge? Have you ever truly sought it with the desperation of a servant expecting a gift, or have you settled for second-hand information?
?
The same argument that Fakhr used to limit knowledge of God, you may use subconsciously to keep God at a distance. Do you ever tell yourself that He is too transcendent to be known intimately, thereby excusing your own lack of pursuit? How does Sanusi's refutation confront that excuse today?
?
The lesson ends with a Quranic declaration of God's bounty: 'And the bounty of Allah He gives to whom He wills.' Are you living in a way that positions you among those who might receive such a bounty? What would change if you truly believed that knowledge of God could be given to you directly?

Refuting Fakhr al-Din on Knowing God's Essence
Sanusi refutes Fakhr al-Din's argument on knowing God's essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's claim that God's essence is unknowable by challenging the exclusivity of the sources of concepts and affirming God's ability to grant direct knowledge.

Core Idea
God can grant knowledge of His essence directly; human limitations do not bind divine power.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argued that we can only conceive things through three avenues: the senses, inner experience, or primary intellectual axioms. Since God's essence falls outside all three, he concluded it is unknowable. Sanusi responds by first denying that these three exhaust the sources of concepts, and then—even if they did—pointing out that God can create necessary knowledge in His servants by His grace, bypassing ordinary means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the structure: first Fakhr's argument, then Sanusi's two-pronged refutation.
Focus on the shift from 'Fakhr argues' to 'The objection is'.
2
Identify Fakhr's three sources
List the three ways Fakhr claims concepts are known: senses (example known), inner experience (pain, pleasure), and primary intellect (like the law of non-contradiction). Observe how he uses this to exclude God's essence.
3
Understand Sanusi's first objection
Sanusi begins by denying that these three are exhaustive. He points out that Fakhr's classification rests on a debatable premise: that all concepts are non-acquired (innate). This is not universally accepted.
This is a methodological rebuttal, not a theological one.
4
Understand Sanusi's second objection
Even if we grant the three sources, Sanusi argues that God can create necessary knowledge of something in a person, even if it is not normally known through those sources. This is a direct appeal to divine omnipotence and mercy.
Note the Qur'anic citation: 'And the bounty of God, He gives it to whom He wills.'
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage shows that knowledge of God's essence is not impossible in principle. It can be granted by God as a special favor, transcending the usual cognitive pathways.
This lesson guards against both extreme rationalism (denying any special divine knowledge) and extreme skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الفخرُ أيضاً : بأنَّا لا نتصوَّرُ إلا ما أدركناه بالحس ، ومثاله معلوم ، أو بالوجدان ، كالألم واللذَّةِ ، أو ببديهة العقل ؛ كبسائط القضايا الأوَّليَّةِ ، وهي كقولنا : النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان ، فهذهِ طُرُقُ معرفةِ التصوراتِ ، وماهية الباري تعالى غيرُ مُدرَكة بالحس ولا بالوجدانِ ولا ببديهة العقل ، فليسَتْ مُدرَكةً لنا . والاعتراض : منع حصر مداركِ التصوراتِ فيما ذَكَرَ ؛ لأنه بناءً على رأيه في التصوراتِ أنها كلها غير مكتسبة ، وهو ممنوع . ثم إذا سُلَّمَ أنَّ مِنْ طرقها العلم الضروري.. فأي مانعِ مِنْ أن يخلق الله تعالى لبعض عبيدِهِ علماً ضرورياً بأمرٍ ما لم تجرِ العادة بخلْقِ العلم بمثلِهِ ؟! وفضلُ اللَّهِ يؤتِيهِ مَنْ يَشَاءُ ، والله ذو الفضل العظيم
And Fakhr [al-Din] also argued: We only conceive what we perceive by the senses, and its example is known, or by inner experience (al-wijdān), like pain and pleasure, or by primary intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql), like the simple primary propositions, such as our saying: Negation and affirmation cannot coexist nor both be absent. So these are the ways of knowing concepts (taṣawwurāt), and the essence (māhiyya) of the Creator, exalted, is not perceived by sense, nor by inner experience, nor by primary intellect, so it is not perceived by us. And the objection: Denying the exclusivity of the sources of concepts to what he mentioned; because it is based on his view that all concepts are non-acquired (ghayr muktasaba), which is denied. Then if it is granted that among its ways is necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī)… so what prevents God, exalted, from creating for some of His servants necessary knowledge of something whose like is not customarily created? And the bounty of God, He gives it to whom He wills, and God is the possessor of great bounty.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. First, it presents Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's argument: we only know concepts through three ways—sensory perception, inner experience (like pain), and primary intellectual axioms (like the law of non-contradiction). Since God's essence is not known through any of these, it cannot be conceived. Then Sanusi objects: the first step is to deny the claim that these three exhaust the sources of concepts—this is based on Fakhr al-Din's own opinion that all concepts are innate (non-acquired), which is rejected. Moreover, even if one accepts these sources, there is no obstacle for God to bestow necessary knowledge of certain matters directly to some of His servants, breaking the customary pattern. Thus, knowledge of God's essence is possible through divine gift.

Misconception: Some think that human cognitive faculties are the only possible source of knowledge about God, so if God's essence is not grasped by sense, experience, or reason, it must be inaccessible.
Correction: Sanusi shows that God is not limited to these sources; He can directly bestow necessary knowledge on whom He wills, making knowledge of His essence possible through divine grace.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's refutation, what is the main flaw in Fakhr al-Din's argument?
A) Fakhr's three sources are not exhaustive.
B) God's essence is actually known through the senses.
C) Primary intellect is insufficient for knowing God.
D) Fakhr misinterpreted the law of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God's essence is possible through a direct gift from God, not limited by ordinary cognitive sources.

?

?
What does your instinctive sense of distance from Allah reveal about whether you truly believe He is 'the Possessor of great bounty' who gives to whom He wills?
?
Are you content to know about Allah through secondary sources and logical proofs, or do you yearn for the direct, heart-piercing knowledge that can only come as a gift from Him? What would change in your life if you pursued that gift with desperate sincerity?

The Principle of Impossibility of Bare Substances and Its Two Demands
Sanusi states a principle and applies it to derive two theological inferences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single rational principle—the impossibility of a substance being devoid of attributes it can receive—supports two key theological conclusions about the world and God.

Core Idea
One logical rule unlocks both the world's temporality and God's eternal attributes.

Sanusi presents a foundational logical principle that underpins two major arguments in Islamic theology. The principle itself is simple: a substance cannot exist stripped of all attributes it is capable of receiving. But from this principle, Sanusi derives two significant demands: one about the world (it is created), and one about God (His attributes are eternal). Understanding this single principle helps you see the unity of kalam reasoning across different domains.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
Start by reading the opening sentence in Arabic and its translation. Sanusi states the principle as an impossibility: it is impossible for a receptive substance to be devoid of attributes. Notice how he formulates it as a foundational rule.
The word 'qābil' here means the substance that can receive attributes.
2
Identify the two demands
After stating the principle, Sanusi says it yields two demands. Find them: one is about the world (al-shāhid), the other about God (al-ghā’ib). Read how he derives each inference using the principle.
The first demand is 'ḥudūth al-‘ālam' (temporal origination of the world). The second is 'wujūb al-qidam' (necessity of eternity) for God's attributes.
3
See the logical structure
Sanusi explains that we already have a proof for the temporality of the world's attributes (e.g., motion). Using the principle, we infer that the world's essence must also be temporal. Similarly, we have a proof for the eternity of God's essence, so we infer that His attributes are eternal. Notice the symmetrical logic.
The principle is the middle term connecting attributes to essence.
4
Understand the correlation
In the summary, Sanusi states that there is a necessary correlation (talāzum) between any essence and the genera of attributes it receives. Because of that correlation, we can infer from one to the other. Grasping this correlation is key to the whole argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عرق القابلِ عمَّا يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ محال
وبهذا تعرفُ أنَّ استحالة عروّ القابلِ عما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ قاعدة يثبت لنا بها مطلبان :
أحدهما في الشاهد : وهو حدوث العالم ؛ لأنَّهُ لمَّا قامَ البرهانُ على حدوثِ صفاتِهِ عرفنا منه حدوث ذاتِهِ ؛ لما علمنا من استحالة عراً الذاتِ عمَّا تقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ
والثاني في حقِّ الغائبِ : وهو وجوب القدم لجميع صفاته تعالى ؛ لأنَّهُ لمَّا قامَ البرهان على وجوبِ قدمِهِ جلَّ وعلا .. عرفنا منهُ وجوبَ القدم لكلِّ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى ؛ لما علمنا أيضاً من استحالة عروّ الذات عما تقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ
والحاصلُ : أَنَّهُ لمَّا انعقد التلازم بينَ كلَّ ذاتٍ وبينَ أجناس مقبولِها مِنَ الصفاتِ . . صح أنْ يُستدَلَّ بما عُلِمَ مِنْ حدوثِ أحدهما على حدوث الآخر ، كما يُستدَلُّ بما عُلِمَ مِنْ قدم أحدهما على قدم الآخرِ
The occurrence of the receptive substance being devoid of what it receives from attributes is impossible. And by this you know that the impossibility of a receptive substance being devoid of what it receives from attributes is a principle by which two demands are established for us: One of them is in the witnessed [realm]: it is the temporal origination of the world; because when the proof for the temporal origination of its attributes was established, we knew from it the temporal origination of its essence, due to our knowledge of the impossibility of the essence being devoid of what it receives from attributes. And the second is regarding the absent [realm]: it is the necessity of eternity for all of His attributes, exalted; because when the proof for the necessity of His eternity, majestic and exalted, was established, we knew from it the necessity of eternity for every attribute of His attributes, exalted; also due to our knowledge of the impossibility of the essence being devoid of what it receives from attributes. And the summary: that when the correlation was established between every essence and the genera of what it receives from attributes, it is valid to infer from the known temporality of one of them the temporality of the other, just as it is valid to infer from the known eternity of one of them the eternity of the other.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of the impossibility of a substance (or essence) being devoid of all attributes it is receptive of, and then applies it to two cases. The author first states the principle as a given: it is impossible for a receptive substance to be empty of the attributes it can accept. Then he says: through this principle we know that this impossibility is a foundational rule (qā‘idah) that yields two demands (maṭlabān). The first demand is in the witnessed realm (al-shāhid), meaning the physical world. Since a proof has already established that the world's attributes (like motion and rest) are temporally originated (ḥādith), we infer from the temporality of its attributes the temporality of the world's essence (dhāt). This inference is valid because we know it is impossible for the essence to be devoid of those attributes. The second demand is regarding the absent realm (al-ghā’ib), meaning the divine. Since a proof has already established the necessity of God's eternity (qidam), we infer from the eternity of His essence the eternity of all His attributes. Again, the same principle applies: it is impossible for His essence to be devoid of His attributes. The author then summarizes: because there is a necessary correlation (talāzum) between any essence and the genera of attributes it can receive, it is valid to use knowledge of one to infer the other, whether for temporality or eternity.

Key Terms
استحالة عرو القابل
المطلبان
الشاهد
الغائب
حدوث العالم
وجوب القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the principle only applies to physical substances, not to God.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly applies the same principle to God in the second demand, showing it is a universal logical rule.

Checkpoint
What is the principle that Sanusi uses to infer both the temporality of the world and the eternity of God's attributes?
A) The impossibility of a substance being devoid of all attributes it can receive.
B) The necessity of a substance having at least one attribute.
C) The correlation between an essence and a specific attribute.
D) The proof of temporal origination from motion.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a substance being devoid of its receptive attributes serves as a universal logical principle that connects the temporality of the world's attributes to its essence and the eternity of God's essence to His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the principle says you cannot be devoid of attributes, what does that mean for your constant state of being before God? Are you aware that at every moment you are a ‘receptive substance’ receiving attributes from your Creator? How would your day change if you remembered that you are never ‘empty’ but always filled with His provision and decree?
?
The same logic that proves the world is temporary also proves God's attributes are eternal. Do you live as if God's knowledge, power, and mercy are truly eternal and present with you right now? When you sin or forget Him, are you acting as though His attributes are absent? What would it look like to truly believe that His eternal attributes are always with you?
?
The passage shows that from what is known about the world you can infer truths about God. What does your own temporality and dependence tell you about the One you depend on? Have you ever used your own weakness and need as a proof that God is strong and self-sufficient? Take a moment to list three ways your neediness points directly to God's perfection.
