The Objection of Temporal Necessity
Objection and initial reply on temporal vs absolute necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between temporal necessity and absolute necessity, and why the objection that actions only prove the former fails to undermine the proof of God's essential attributes.

Core Idea
Actions only show temporary necessity, but the proof needs absolute, eternal necessity.

The opponent claims that actions (like creation) only prove that God possesses attributes temporarily at the moment of action, not eternally. Sanusi begins his response by pointing out that even if true, this conclusion is too weak—the opponent's own proof would produce a general result that does not establish their claim. Let's follow the first stage of this dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening objection
Read the first Arabic segment carefully. The opponent states that actions only indicate temporal necessity (wujūb waqtī) for the agent's attributes at the time of the act.
Notice the phrase 'وجوباً وقتيّاً' as a key term.
2
Contrast temporal vs. absolute necessity
Sanusi accepts that actions show necessity at the time of action, but he argues that this is not the absolute necessity (wujūb muṭlaq) needed to prove the impossibility of God being devoid of attributes eternally.
Absolute necessity means necessary according to the essence, at all times.
3
See the logical flaw
Sanusi states that absolute temporality is more general than absolute necessity, and the implication of the more general does not require the more specific. Thus the opponent's proof only yields a general conclusion, not their specific claim.
The phrase 'ملزوم الأعم غير ملزوم الأخص' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القولُ باستحالة عروه عن الأوصاف المذكورة حتى يتم لكم استدلالكم بها على استحالة عروه عن سائر صفاتِهِ
قولكم في دليل استحالة عروه عن الأوصاف المذكورة : ( إِنَّ فعلهُ الموجود دلَّ عليها مِنْ حيثُ توقف إيجادِهِ الاختياري على اتصافه بتلك الصفاتِ المذكورة ) ، نقولُ : إنَّما يدلُّ الفعلُ على وجوب اتصافِ فاعله بتلك الصفاتِ وجوباً وقتيّاً ؛ أعني : وقت إيجادِهِ ذلكَ الفعل . لا وجوباً مطلقاً بحسَبِ الذاتِ ، والذي يُوجِبُ استحالة العرو الثاني لا الأوَّلُ ؛ لما عُلِمَ أنَّ الوقتيَّة المطلقة أعمُّ مِنَ الضروريَّةِ المطلقة ، وملزوم الأعم غير ملزوم الأخص
والحاصلُ : أَنَّ الذي أنتجه دليلكم أعمُّ مِنْ مدَّعاكم
جوابه : منعُ أَنَّ الأفعالَ إِنَّما دلَّتْ على وجوبِ تلك الصفات لفاعلها وجوباً وقتيّاً ، بل وجوباً مطلقاً بحسَبِ الذاتِ ، بحيثُ يستحيلُ عرو الفاعل عن تلك الصفات مطلقاً
وبيانُ ذلكَ : أنَّهُ لو قُدِّرَ الجواز لتلك الصفاتِ لكانَتْ مِنْ جملة الأفعال الحادثة ؛ ضرورةَ أنَّ كلَّ ممكن حادث ، فيجب أن يتّصفَ فاعلُها بأمثالها ؛ ليتمكَّنَ بها مِنْ إيجادها ، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى تلكَ الصفاتِ الأُخر ، فإنْ قُدْرَ لها الجواز أيضاً لزم حدوثها أيضاً ، ولزم اتصافُ فاعلها بأمثالها ؛ ليتمكَّنَ بها أيضاً مِنْ إيجادِها ، ولزم الدورُ إِنْ كانَتْ هذه الصفاتُ هي الأولى ، أو التسلسل إنْ كانَتْ غيرَها ! فإذاً ؛ الأفعالُ لا يمكن صدورها عن فاعل تكون تلك الصفاتُ في حقه جائزة
لا يُقالُ : نحنُ إنَّما اعترضنا على استدلالكم على وجوبها بمجرَّدِ الفعل ، وهذا الذي أجبتُم بهِ لم يصح الاستدلال بهِ على ذلك ، بل حاصله : استنباط دليل آخر على وجوبِ تلك الصفاتِ للفاعل ؛ وهو أنَّها لو كانَتْ جائزةً للزمَ الدورُ أو التسلسل ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : إِنَّما استلزم جواز تلك الصفاتِ الدور أو التسلسل من حيثُ إِنَّ كلَّ جائز لا يكون إلا فعلاً حادثاً ، والفعلُ الحادث يدلُّ على تلك الصفاتِ ، ثم ننقل الكلام إليها ، ويلزم الدورُ أو التسلسل .
The statement about the impossibility of His being devoid of the mentioned attributes until your argument is complete for you by it regarding the impossibility of His being devoid of His other attributes. Your statement in the proof of the impossibility of His being devoid of the mentioned attributes: (Verily, His existing action indicates them by virtue of the dependence of his voluntary bringing into existence on His being described with those mentioned attributes), we say: The action indicates the necessity of the agent's being described with those attributes only as temporal necessity; I mean: at the time of his bringing that action into existence. Not absolute necessity according to the essence. And what necessitates the impossibility of being devoid is the second, not the first; because it is known that absolute temporality is more general than absolute necessity, and the implication of the more general is not the implication of the more specific. And the conclusion: That which your proof produced is more general than your claim. His answer: Denial that actions only indicate the necessity of those attributes for their agent as temporal necessity, but rather as absolute necessity according to the essence, such that the agent's being devoid of those attributes is impossible absolutely. And the explanation of that: That if permissibility were supposed for those attributes, they would be among the originated acts; necessarily because every possible is originated. So their agent must be described with their likes in order to be able by them to bring them into existence. Then we transfer the discourse to those other attributes. If permissibility for them is also supposed, their origination also follows, and the description of their agent with their likes follows in order to be able by them also to bring them into existence. And circularity follows if these attributes are the first, or infinite regress if they are other than them! So then: Actions cannot proceed from an agent with respect to whom those attributes are permissible. It is not said: We only objected to your argument for their necessity by mere action, and what you have answered with is not a valid argument for that; rather its conclusion is derivation of another proof for the necessity of those attributes for the agent; which is that if they were permissible, circularity or infinite regress would follow. For we say: The permissibility of those attributes necessitates circularity or infinite regress only because every permissible is nothing but an originated act, and the originated act indicates those attributes, then we transfer the discourse to them, and circularity or infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the proof that God's essential attributes (such as knowledge, power, will) are necessary for Him. The objection claims that actions (like creation) only prove that God possesses these attributes temporarily at the moment of action, not that He possesses them eternally and absolutely. The author (Sanusi) first summarizes the opponent's argument: they say that a voluntary act depends on the agent being described with certain attributes at the time of the act, but this only shows temporal necessity, not an essential, eternal necessity. The author then counters that if only temporal necessity is required, then the opponent's proof does not establish what they claim—it only proves that God has attributes at the moment of action, not that He cannot be devoid of them essentially. The author then provides a direct answer: actions actually prove absolute necessity, because if the attributes were merely possible (i.e., God could lack them), they would themselves be originated acts (since all possibilities are originated). But then these attributes would require other attributes in the agent to bring them about, leading to an infinite regress or circularity if the same attributes are repeated. Therefore, attributes must be necessary for the agent eternally. The passage also anticipates a further objection: that this new argument is not the same as the original proof; the author responds that it is an independent demonstration that attributes cannot be merely permissible. The argument is presented in a dialectical style typical of kalam.

Key Terms
وجوب وقتي (temporal necessity)
وجوب مطلق (absolute necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if actions prove necessity for attributes, it must be absolute necessity. But the objector argues it is only temporary.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that actions indeed only show temporal necessity; the opponent's own proof is too weak for their claim.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the action prove about the agent's attributes?
A) Absolute necessity according to the essence
B) Temporal necessity at the time of action
C) That the attributes are impossible
D) That the attributes are possible

Key Takeaway
Actions alone prove temporal necessity, not the absolute necessity needed to show the impossibility of God being devoid of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself satisfied with a faith that only rises to the occasion when you need God's help, forgetting that His attributes are eternal and not just reactions to your circumstances? How might you cultivate a recognition of His absolute necessity beyond your temporal needs?
?
When you claim God is All-Knowing or All-Powerful, do you truly believe these attributes are essential to His nature at every moment, or do you secretly think of them only when you experience His actions? How would embracing the absolute necessity of His attributes change your reliance on Him?

Absolute Necessity Through Regress
Argument from impossibility of regress to prove absolute necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's attributes must be absolutely necessary because if they were merely possible, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
What is merely possible is originated; each originated act requires attributes, leading to an impossible chain.

Sanusi now presents his own direct argument to prove absolute necessity. He assumes the opposite—that attributes are merely possible—and shows that this leads to an absurd consequence: each possible attribute would itself be an originated act requiring further attributes, resulting in either circularity or infinite regress. This clinches the refutation, and he then answers a final objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read from 'جوابه' to 'فإذاً'. Sanusi claims that actions prove absolute necessity, not just temporal. Follow his reasoning.
Pay attention to the terms 'جائز' (possible) and 'حادث' (originated).
2
Follow the regress chain
Sanusi argues: if attributes were merely possible (ja'iz), they would be originated acts. Each originated act requires the agent to have certain attributes to bring it about. Those attributes would again be possible, leading to another set of acts, and so on. This results in either circularity (if the same attributes are needed again) or infinite regress (if different attributes).
The key is 'دور' (circularity) and 'تسلسل' (infinite regress).
3
Understand the final exchange
Read the 'لا يُقال' section. The opponent objects that this is a new argument, not the original proof. Sanusi responds that it is still a valid proof against the possibility of attributes.
Notice how Sanusi reaffirms that the reasoning is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القولُ باستحالة عروه عن الأوصاف المذكورة حتى يتم لكم استدلالكم بها على استحالة عروه عن سائر صفاتِهِ
قولكم في دليل استحالة عروه عن الأوصاف المذكورة : ( إِنَّ فعلهُ الموجود دلَّ عليها مِنْ حيثُ توقف إيجادِهِ الاختياري على اتصافه بتلك الصفاتِ المذكورة ) ، نقولُ : إنَّما يدلُّ الفعلُ على وجوب اتصافِ فاعله بتلك الصفاتِ وجوباً وقتيّاً ؛ أعني : وقت إيجادِهِ ذلكَ الفعل . لا وجوباً مطلقاً بحسَبِ الذاتِ ، والذي يُوجِبُ استحالة العرو الثاني لا الأوَّلُ ؛ لما عُلِمَ أنَّ الوقتيَّة المطلقة أعمُّ مِنَ الضروريَّةِ المطلقة ، وملزوم الأعم غير ملزوم الأخص
والحاصلُ : أَنَّ الذي أنتجه دليلكم أعمُّ مِنْ مدَّعاكم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the need for attributes in an agent only applies to originated beings, not to God. But Sanusi shows that if attributes were merely possible, they would be originated and thus prove the need.
Correction: The argument works by assuming the attribute itself is possible, which would make it an originated act, then the same reasoning applies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if God's attributes are assumed to be merely possible (ja'iz)?
A) They would be eternal and necessary.
B) They would be originated acts, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
C) They would prove that God does not exist.
D) They would be impossible for God to possess.

Key Takeaway
If God's attributes were merely possible, each would be an originated act requiring further attributes, leading to circularity or infinite regress, so they must be absolutely necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes were merely possible, His knowledge could change or fail. How does that truth—that His attributes are absolutely necessary—force you to reconsider any area of your life where you doubt His promises or power? What will you do today to act on His absolute certainty?
?
The argument shows that without necessary attributes, we cannot account for any action. Do you live as if God's attributes are only 'temporal necessaries'—needed when you're in trouble—or as absolutely necessary for every breath? What would change in your morning routine if you truly believed His power is not contingent?
?
Sanusi refutes the idea that actions indicate only temporal necessity. When do you treat God's qualities as temporary relief rather than eternal truths? How can you shift your reliance from hoping God will act to knowing He must act according to His necessary attributes?

The Four Meanings of al-Daruri and Their Applicability to God
Author distinguishes four meanings of 'necessary' and identifies which is impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct meanings of the term 'daruri' (necessary) in Islamic theology and why only one of them is impossible for God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Not every 'necessary' is impossible for God; only necessity from harm and need is.

The term 'daruri' (necessary) is used in several distinct ways in Islamic thought, and confusing them can lead to theological error. In this passage, the author carefully distinguishes four meanings and identifies which one cannot apply to Allah's knowledge. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the four meanings and their explanation.
Pay attention to how the author structures the enumeration and the glosses.
2
Identify the four categories
Locate each of the four meanings of 'daruri' mentioned. Notice that the first is about power, the second and third about knowledge, and the fourth about need and harm.
The first meaning is not restricted to knowledge; it applies to actions too.
3
Study the glosses
Read the two glosses from al-Manjur. They provide concrete examples for the first meaning and clarify the relationship between the second and third meanings.
The third meaning is more general than the second.
4
Understand the theological conclusion
Focus on why the fourth meaning is impossible for God: because it implies need and harm, which are incompatible with God's perfection. The first three meanings are not impossible.
This is why the term 'daruri' is not applied to God's knowledge.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can identify the meaning that is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضروري يطلق على أربعة معانٍ : ما ليس بمقدور بالقدرة الحادثة، ونقيضُه المكتسب؛ وهو المقدور بها، وهذا لا يختص بالعلم، بل يُقالُ : حركة ضرورية (١) أي : غير مقدورة بالقدرة الحادثة الثاني : ما عُلِمَ بغير دليل الثالثُ : ما عُلِمَ مِنْ غيرِ تقدُّمِ نظر (۳)، وهذان مُختصَّانِ بالعلوم . الرابع : ما قارنه ضررٌ وحاجةٌ ؛ كعلم الإنسانِ جوعَهُ وألمَهُ ، وهذا المعنى الأخيرُ هو المستحيل في حقٌّ علم الباري جلَّ وعلا ، دونَ المعاني الثلاثةِ ، ولأجلهِ امتنع إطلاق لفظ الضروري عليهِ (۱) قال العلامة المنجور في ( حاشيته على شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ( ص ١٣٤ ) : ( كحركة المرتعش والمسحوب والساقط من علو ؛ إذ كل هذه الحركات معجوز عنها ، لا قدرة عليها ) (۲) قال العلامة المنجور في ( حاشيته على شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ( ص ١٣٤ ) : ) الثالث أعم من الثاني ؛ لأنَّ ما علم بغير تقدم نظر قد يعلم بغير دليل ، وقد يعلم بدليل يدل ضرورة ولا يتوقف في دلالته على نظر ، كما مرَّ أن الإحكام يدل على العلم بالضرورة ، والاختيار إنما يدل على العلم بالنظر ) .
Al-daruri (the necessary) is applied in four meanings: (1) That which is not possible by created power, and its opposite is the acquired (al-muktasab), which is possible by it. This is not specific to knowledge, rather it is said: necessary movement (1) i.e., not possible by created power. (2) That which is known without a proof. (3) That which is known without prior consideration (nazar). These two are specific to knowledge. (4) That which is accompanied by harm and need, like a person's knowledge of his hunger and pain. This last meaning is impossible regarding the knowledge of the Creator, Glorified and Exalted, unlike the first three meanings, and because of it, applying the term 'necessary' to Him is prohibited. (1) The scholar al-Manjur said in his gloss on the explanation of the Great Creed (p. 134): 'Like the movement of the trembling person, the dragged one, and the one falling from a height; for all these movements are beyond one's capability, one has no power over them.' (2) The scholar al-Manjur said in his gloss (p. 134): 'The third is more general than the second; because what is known without prior consideration may be known without a proof, and may be known by a proof that indicates necessarily and does not depend on consideration in its indication, as it has been mentioned that perfection indicates knowledge necessarily, whereas choice indicates knowledge by consideration.'
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage clarifies the term 'daruri' (necessary) and its applicability to God's knowledge. It begins by stating that 'daruri' is used in four distinct senses: (1) What is not within the scope of created power — its opposite is 'acquired' (muktasab). This applies not only to knowledge but also to actions like involuntary movements. (2) What is known without any proof (dallil). (3) What is known without prior intellectual consideration (tqaddum nazar). The second and third are restricted to knowledge, and the third is broader than the second. (4) What is accompanied by harm and need, such as a person's awareness of their own hunger or pain. The author explains that the last meaning is impossible for God's knowledge because God is free from need and harm. The first three meanings, however, are not impossible for God. Therefore, the term 'daruri' is not applied to God. The passage includes two glosses from al-Manjur illustrating the first meaning with examples of involuntary movement, and explaining the relationship between the second and third meanings.

Key Terms
ضروري
مكتسب
نظر
دليل
حركة ضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'necessary' in any sense applies to God, but only the meaning involving need is impossible.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the fourth meaning (accompanied by harm and need) is impossible for God; the other three are not.

Checkpoint
Which of the four meanings of 'daruri' is impossible for God's knowledge according to the passage?
A) What is not within created power
B) What is known without proof
C) What is known without prior consideration
D) What is accompanied by harm and need

Key Takeaway
Only the meaning of 'necessary' that implies harm or need is impossible for Allah's knowledge; the other meanings are not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience an involuntary reaction like trembling from cold or fear, do you recognize it as a sign that your power is limited? How does that realization affect your reliance on Allah in moments of weakness?
?
You use words like 'necessary' to describe Allah's attributes. After this lesson, how will you guard against letting those words carry unintended meanings of need or harm? What specific terms will you be more careful with?
?
The author says that the fourth meaning (knowledge accompanied by harm/need) is impossible for Allah. Yet you often turn to Allah in times of pain and need. How can your supplication be genuine while affirming that Allah knows your need without being affected by it?

Three Proofs That Command is Distinct from Will
Sanusi enumerates three proofs for the distinction between command and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs from the Ash'ari scholars demonstrating that God's command (amr) is a distinct attribute from His will (irada).

Core Idea
Command and will are distinct: God commands what He does not will.

How do we know that God's command to do something is not the same as Him willing it? The Ash'ari scholars (al-ashab) presented three clear proofs from revelation and reason. In this passage, Sanusi summarizes these proofs, each of which shows that a command can exist without the corresponding will. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi presents three distinct arguments.
The proofs are numbered in the text.
2
Analyze the first proof
The first proof uses the case of God commanding disbelievers to believe without willing their belief. Understand the logical implication: if God willed it, it would happen; since it doesn't, He doesn't will it.
This relies on the principle that God's will is always effective.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof contrasts the object of command (act of another) with the object of will (act of the one willing). See why this shows they are different.
Will here means volition, not desire or love.
4
Analyze the third proof
The third proof uses an oath example to show that a command can exist without the will, because otherwise the oath would be broken.
Consensus (ijma') establishes that the oath is not broken.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand how all three proofs together establish the distinction, and why this matters for theology.
This distinction preserves divine justice and the nature of human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الأصحابُ على مغايرته للإرادة بوجودِ الأمر بدونِها ، وبيَّنوه بوجوه (۱) :
الأوّلُ : أَنَّ الله تعالى أمر الكفَّارَ بالإيمان والعصاة بالطاعة ، ولم يُرِدْ ذلك منهم ؛ إذ لو أرادَ ذلكَ لوقعَ ، وإلا لزمَ النقصُ بنفوذ مشيئة العبدِ بدونِ مشيئة الله تعالى ، وقدِ اتَّفق السلفُ قبل ظهور البدع على أنَّ ما شاءَ اللهُ كانَ ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن .
الثاني : أنَّ الأمر يتعلَّق بفعل الغيرِ ، والإرادة ـ لا بمعنى الشهوة والمحبة (۲) ـ لا تتعلق إلا بفعل المريد
الثالثُ : أَنَّ مَنْ حَلَفَ : ليقضينَ غريمَهُ دينَهُ غداً إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ ، فتمكَّنَ مِنْ قضائِهِ ولم يقضه.. لم يحنث ، معَ أَنَّ اللهَ قد أمرَهُ بذلك (۳) ، فلو تضمَّنَ الأمرُ الإرادة لكانَ قد شاءَ اللهُ قضاءَهُ ، فكانَ يجب أن يحنث ، ولم يحنث بالإجماع
And the colleagues (al-aṣḥāb) argued for its being different from will by the existence of command without it, and they explained it with several aspects:
First: that God Most High commanded the disbelievers to believe and the sinners to obey, and He did not will that from them; because if He had willed it, it would have occurred; otherwise, deficiency (naqṣ) would result from the penetration of the will of the servant without the will of God Most High. The Salaf before the emergence of innovations agreed that what God wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be.
Second: that the command (amr) relates to the act of another, while will (irāda) — not in the sense of desire (shahwa) and love (maḥabba) — only relates to the act of the one willing (murīd).
Third: that if someone swears: 'By Allah I will pay my debtor's debt tomorrow if God wills,' and he is able to pay it but does not pay it, he does not break his oath (lā yahnath), even though God has commanded him to do so. Now if the command comprised will, then God would have willed its payment, and consequently he would have broken his oath, but he does not break his oath by consensus.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujūh) from the Ashʿarī scholars (al-aṣḥāb) demonstrating that command (amr) is distinct from will (irāda). The first proof: God commands the disbelievers to believe and the sinners to obey, but He does not will their faith and obedience from them. If He had willed it, it would have occurred, because nothing can happen without His will. If someone claims that God wills it but it doesn't happen, that would imply deficiency (naqṣ) because the will of the servant would be effective without God's will. The Salaf agreed that God's will is always effective. Therefore, the command to disbelievers shows that command exists without will, so they are different. The second proof: command relates to the act of another (one commands someone else to do something), while will (in the sense of intention or volition) relates only to the act of the one willing. So they have different objects. The third proof uses an oath example: a person swears to pay his creditor's debt tomorrow if God wills, but then he is able but does not pay. He does not break his oath (by consensus), although God commanded him to pay. If command were identical to will, then God would have willed the payment, so it would be a valid withholding of what God willed, making the oath broken. The existence of a command without the will shows they are distinct. So these three proofs establish that command is distinct from will.

Key Terms
الأمر
الإرادة
مشيئة
الحنث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God commands something, He must also will it to happen.
Correction: The first proof shows that God commands disbelievers to believe yet does not will their belief, because if He willed it, it would occur. Therefore, command does not entail will.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does God not will the faith of the disbelievers despite commanding them?
A) Because if He willed it, it would have occurred, and the fact that it does not occur shows He does not will it.
B) Because the disbelievers have free will to choose.
C) Because God's command is only a test, not a genuine demand.
D) Because the disbelievers are commanded only outwardly.

Key Takeaway
God's command and will are distinct attributes; He may command something without willing its occurrence, as shown by the case of the disbelievers and other proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that nothing in your life occurs except by God's will—including your failures? The first proof says if God wills it, it must happen. Does your heart submit to this, or do you complain when things don't go your way?
?
God commands you to obey Him, but He may not will your obedience. Does this frighten you? It means your obedience is not coerced—you are choosing in a way that God has not predestined for you. How should this shape your gratitude and effort?
?
When you say 'insha'Allah' before a plan, do you use it as a mere cultural phrase, or do you truly acknowledge that God's will may differ from your command? The oath example shows that God's command can exist without His will to bring it about. Does your 'insha'Allah' reflect that humble surrender?

Ibn al-Tilmisani's Critique of the First Proof
Ibn al-Tilmisani argues that the impossibility of infinite temporal things does not apply to the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn al-Tilmisani argues that the first proof against infinite temporal events does not refute Abu Sahl's claim of eternal sciences.

Core Idea
Proofs against infinite temporal events do not apply to the eternal.

Ibn al-Tilmisani examines the first proof used by the majority against Abu Sahl's doctrine. He argues that the proof's logic depends on features unique to temporal things, such as having a beginning and being subject to termination. Since Abu Sahl's sciences are said to be eternal, the proof fails. He then points to the second proof—consensus—as the stronger argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from critique to conclusion.
The passage includes specific arguments in parentheses.
2
Identify why the first proof is problematic
Ibn al-Tilmisani states that the proof only shows impossibility for temporal events, not for the eternal. Identify this key point.
3
List the specific arguments he mentions
He enumerates reasons like 'cutting off' and 'proportion of two sets'. Notice each one and why it doesn't carry over to the eternal.
These arguments rely on temporal features like being preceded by non-existence.
4
Understand why they don't apply to the eternal
The eternal does not have a beginning or an end, so arguments assuming termination or non-existence preceding are irrelevant.
5
Note his conclusion to rely on consensus
He does not dismiss the refutation entirely but recommends the second proof (consensus) as the correct method.
This shows methodological flexibility within the Ash'ari school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْسانيّ : ( والردُّ الأَوَّلُ فيهِ نظر ؛ فإِنَّ الذي قام الدليلُ على استحالتِهِ : وجود حوادث لا نهاية لها ، وبيَّنوا الاستحالة فيها بوجوه لا تطَّردُ معَ فَرْضِ القدم ؛ كتقديرِ خروج بعضها عن الجملة (١) ، ونسبة الجملتين ، ولزوم تطرُّقِ الأقل والأكثر لما لا يتناهى ، فإِنَّ فَرْضَ نفْي الواجب محال ، بخلاف الحادثِ وكذلك الاستدلال بالجمْعِ بين عدم النهاية والانقضاء لا يطرد هنا ؛ لوجوبها (۲) ، وكذلك الاستدلال بأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مسبوق بعدمِ نفسِهِ (1). فالكل مسبوق بالعدمِ ، كلُّ ذلك لا يمكن تقريره هنا ) قال : ( فالوجه في الردّ : الاعتماد على الوجه الثاني ؛ وهو الإجماع ( انتهى (١)
Ibn al-Tilmisānī said: (The first refutation is open to consideration; for that which the proof has established its impossibility is the existence of infinite [temporal] events, and they demonstrated the impossibility for them in ways that do not extend consistently to the hypothesis of eternality—such as postulating the separation of some individuals from the set (1), and the proportion of two sets, and the necessity of the lesser and greater applying to what has no end. For hypothesizing the negation of the Necessary is impossible, unlike the temporal. Likewise, the proof by combining infinity and termination does not extend here, because of their necessity (2). Likewise the proof that every individual is preceded by its own non-existence, so all are preceded by non-existence—all of that cannot be established here.) He said: (The correct method in the refutation is to rely on the second proof—which is consensus.) (Ended) (1)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the first proof given by the majority against Abu Sahl. Ibn al-Tilmisānī points out that the proof which demonstrates the impossibility of an infinite regress (an infinite series of events) applies specifically to temporal things (ḥawādith). The arguments used—such as the 'cutting off' (khurūj baʿḍihā ʿan al-jumla) or the comparison of two infinite sets—rely on features unique to temporal entities, like being preceded by non-existence, having a beginning, and being subject to termination. These features do not apply to the eternal (al-qadīm). Therefore, the first proof does not necessarily refute Abu Sahl's position, because he posits eternal sciences, not temporal ones. However, Ibn al-Tilmisānī does not dismiss the refutation entirely; he concludes that the second proof, which is based on consensus (ijmāʿ), is sound and should be relied upon. This shows the internal methodological rigor within the Ashʿarī school: even a proof that is weak in one respect can be set aside in favor of a stronger one.

Key Terms
ابن التلمساني
الرد الأول
الحوادث
القدم
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All arguments against an infinite regress apply equally to eternal and temporal series.
Correction: Ibn al-Tilmisani specifies that these arguments only apply to temporal things because they rely on features like having a beginning and being preceded by non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn al-Tilmisānī consider the first proof (against infinite entities) inconclusive against Abu Sahl?
A) Because Abu Sahl's sciences are said to be temporal.
B) Because the arguments rely on features unique to temporal things, not the eternal.
C) Because the majority did not provide enough evidence.
D) Because consensus is a weaker argument.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Tilmisani demonstrates that the first proof against infinite regress is insufficient because it relies on temporal properties; instead, consensus (ijmā') provides the proper refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a widely accepted religious argument, do you examine it critically like Ibn al-Tilmisānī, or do you accept it because it is common? What is one belief you hold that might crumble if you applied the same scrutiny?
?
Ibn al-Tilmisānī set aside a weak proof and trusted the consensus of scholars. In your own life, which justifications do you cling to that may be weaker than the collective wisdom of the believing community? What would change if you truly submitted to that consensus?
?
The first proof failed because it projected temporal limitations onto the eternal. Have you ever thought of Allah's attributes in human terms—like being limited by time or space? How can you train your mind to remember that He is not like His creation?

Do Non-Visual Perceptions Apply to All Existents?
Sanusi reports the disagreement on the generality of non-visual perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among early Sunni theologians on whether perceptions other than vision can encompass all existents, and why Sanusi follows al-Ash'ari's position.

Core Idea
Perceptions beyond vision may or may not apply to all things – a key debate in Ash'ari theology.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin introduces a foundational disagreement among early Sunni theologians about the scope of human perceptions. While some held that only vision can perceive any existent, al-Ash'ari argued that all five senses can in principle perceive any object. Sanusi explicitly aligns himself with al-Ash'ari, setting the stage for later discussions on divine attributes and the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice the key figures and their positions.
The passage is dense; focus on identifying the two opposing views.
2
Identify the two main positions
Locate the view of al-Kullabi and al-Qalanisi (perceptions other than vision do not apply to all existents) and the view of al-Ash'ari (all perceptions apply to all existents).
Note that al-Ash'ari's position is the one Sanusi follows.
3
Analyze the author's stance
Sanusi states he follows al-Ash'ari's school. Consider why this matters for the rest of the creed.
This sets a precedent for later discussions on hearing and seeing God.
4
Apply the disagreement
Ask yourself: what difference does it make whether non-visual perceptions can apply to all existents? How does this affect theology?
The answer will be explored in the next lesson on hearing and eternal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في جواز تعلَّقِ ما عدا الرؤيةَ مِنَ الإدراكات بكل موجود ؛ فذهب القدماء منهم ؛ كعبدِ الله بن سعيد الكلابي والقلانسي : إلى أنَّ هذا العموم مختص بالرؤيةِ ، وبقية الإدراكات لا يجوز أن تعم الموجوداتِ ، ونُقِلَ عن الشيخ أبي الحسنِ مخالفتهما في ذلك ، وصارَ إلى جواز عموم كلّ إدراك لكل موجود ، ومذهب الشيخ أبي الحسنِ إمامِ أهلِ السنَّةِ وإليهِ يُنسبون سلكتُ في هذه العقيدة . ونُقِلَ عن عبدِ اللهِ بنِ سعيدٍ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا خص تعلق السمعِ بالأصواتِ ذهب إلى أنَّ الكلامَ الأزليَّ لا يصح أن يُسمَعَ ؛ يعني والله أعلمُ : بل يُدرَكُ بصفةِ العلم ، وفي قولِهِ ذلكَ مخالفة لقواطع السمعِ والشيخ أبو الحسن رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ لمَّا قالَ : ( إدراك السمع يعم كلَّ موجود ( جوّزَ تعلقه بكلام الله تعالى ، وقال بوقوع هذا الجائز على ما ورد السمع به في حقٌّ موسى عليه السلام ، وعمدة الشيخ في ذلك : ما يأتي تقريرُهُ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى في فصل الرؤيةِ ؛ مِنْ أَنَّ الوجود هو المصحح للرؤية .
And they differed concerning the permissibility of the attachment of perceptions other than vision to every existent. The early ones among them, such as Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi and al-Qalanisi, held that this generality is specific to vision, and the rest of the perceptions are not permissible to encompass existents. And it was transmitted from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) that he disagreed with them in that, and he held the permissibility of the generality of every perception for every existent. And the school of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, the Imam of the People of the Sunna, to whom they are attributed, I have followed in this creed. And it was transmitted from Abdullah ibn Sa'id that when he specified the attachment of hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech (al-kalam al-azali) is not valid to be heard; meaning, and God knows best, rather it is perceived by the attribute of knowledge. And in that statement of his there is opposition to decisive proofs of revelation (qawati' al-sam'). And Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, may God be pleased with him, when he said: 'The perception of hearing encompasses every existent,' he allowed its attachment to the speech of God Almighty, and he said that this possible thing occurs according to what revelation has brought concerning Moses, peace be upon him. And the proof of the Shaykh in that is what will come, God willing, in the chapter on vision, that existence is the validator for vision.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents a disagreement among early Sunni theologians about whether perceptions (idrakat) other than vision can apply to all existents. The author, following al-Ash'ari, reports that the earlier figures al-Kullabi and al-Qalanisi held that only vision has the general scope, while other perceptions like hearing, touch, etc., are limited to specific objects. Al-Ash'ari disagreed and held that every perception can in principle apply to any existent. The author explicitly states that he follows al-Ash'ari's school. The passage then discusses the specific case of hearing: al-Kullabi, by restricting hearing to sounds, concluded that God's eternal speech (kalam azali) cannot be heard; instead it is known by the attribute of knowledge. Al-Ash'ari, because he holds that hearing can apply to any existent, allows that God's speech can be heard, and that this actually occurred for Moses. The foundation of al-Ash'ari's position is that existence itself is what makes something perceptible, a point to be elaborated later in the chapter on vision. The author notes that al-Kullabi's position contradicts clear scriptural evidence (qawati' al-sam').

Key Terms
إدراكات
عموم
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the view of al-Kullabi and al-Qalanisi regarding perceptions other than vision?
A) They held that all perceptions can apply to all existents.
B) They held that only vision can apply to all existents.
C) They held that no perception applies to God.
D) They held that hearing can apply to all existents.

Key Takeaway
The scope of perceptions is a debated issue in kalam; al-Ash'ari's inclusive position allows for the possibility of perceiving God in ways beyond vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that only certain senses can perceive God, what does that say about your concept of God's accessibility? Are you unintentionally confining the All-Powerful to human limitations?
?
Al-Ash'ari held that every perception can apply to every existent, including God. How would your worship change if you truly believed that your entire being—not just your eyes—can be directed toward perceiving Him?
?
This lesson presents a theological debate. But if you are honest, have you ever limited God by assuming He can only be known or experienced in one way? What step can you take today to expand your expectation of encountering Him?

Hearing and Eternal Speech: A Specific Application
Sanusi discusses the application of the generality of hearing to God's eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the general principle about perceptions applies to the specific case of hearing God's eternal speech, and why al-Ash'ari allows Moses to have heard it.

Core Idea
If hearing can perceive any existent, then God's eternal speech can be heard.

In this continuation, Sanusi applies the earlier disagreement to a concrete theological issue: Can God's eternal speech (al-kalam al-azali) be heard? Al-Kullabi, who restricts hearing to sounds, concludes that eternal speech cannot be heard; rather it is known by knowledge. Al-Ash'ari, who holds that hearing encompasses every existent, allows that Moses actually heard God's speech. The passage also notes that al-Kullabi's position contradicts clear scriptural proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the specific argument about hearing and eternal speech.
Notice how al-Kullabi's general principle leads to his conclusion about eternal speech.
2
Trace the logical chain
Identify: al-Kullabi's restriction of hearing → eternal speech not hearable; al-Ash'ari's generality → eternal speech can be heard → actually heard by Moses.
The chain is based on the principle of existence as the basis for perceptibility.
3
Understand the scriptural objection
Note that al-Kullabi's view is said to contradict qawati' al-sam' (decisive scriptural proofs). What scriptural evidence might support al-Ash'ari?
The reference is to Moses hearing God's speech (Quran 4:164, 7:143).
4
Preview the foundation
Sanusi mentions al-Ash'ari's proof comes later: existence itself is the validator for perception. Think about what that means.
This anticipates the upcoming chapter on vision.

Why did al-Kullabi hold that eternal speech cannot be heard?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's speech is audible and that Moses heard it, how does that transform your relationship with the Qur'an? Will you listen to it as if you might hear the very voice of God? What one change will you make in how you recite or listen to the Qur'an starting today?
?
Al-Kullabi limited God's speech to what his own reasoning could accommodate. Have you ever made God's attributes fit into your limited framework? What assumption about God's nature do you need to surrender, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The proof that existence validates perception suggests that God could make Himself known to you in unexpected ways. Are you living in expectant hope of encountering God, or have you reduced Him to a concept? What would it mean for you to live as if you might hear Him speak today?

Do We Touch Motion? The Akwan Dispute
Sanusi reports two opinions on whether touch perceives the akwan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disputed question among Ash'ari theologians about whether the accidents of motion and rest are directly perceived by the sense of touch.

Core Idea
Does touch directly perceive motion and rest? Ash'aris disagreed.

In this short passage, Sanusi presents a specific disagreement within the Ash'ari school. While all agree that motion and rest are seen by the eye, the question is whether they are also felt by touch. Two positions emerge, each with its own reasoning. Let's explore them.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the two opposing views and the proof given for the first.
The term 'akwan' is a technical kalam term for the accident class that includes motion, rest, etc.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find where Sanusi states the first opinion ('some held') and the second opinion ('some denied'). Also note the proof offered.
3
Understand the proof for touch
The first group argues that if an object moves under your hand, you perceive its motion by touch. Similarly, if its parts separate, you perceive the separation. This seems intuitive.
They equate direct tactile experience with direct perception of the accident.
4
Consider the counterargument
The second group says you know about the motion or separation at the time of touch, but touch itself does not perceive the accident. It's a distinction between inference and direct perception.
5
Reflect on the significance
This dispute shows how precise kalam is about the senses and their objects. It also illustrates the internal diversity of the Ash'ari school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف الأصحابُ في الأكوان التي هي متعلق للرؤية في وقتنا اتفاقاً : هل هي مُتعلَّق للمس أم لا ؟ فذهبَ بعضُهم إلى أنَّ إدراك اللمس يتعلَّق بها ، واحتج بأنَّ مَنْ لمس شيئاً واضطرب تحت يده أدرك حركته ، وإذا تفرَّقَتْ أجزاؤُهُ في يدِهِ أدركَ تفرقتها ، ومِنَ الأصحاب مَنْ أنكر ذلكَ ، وزعمَ أَنَّهُ يعلم ذلكَ عندَ اللمس ، ولم يتعلَّق إدراك اللَّمسِ بهِ .
And the companions differed concerning the akwan (accidents of motion/rest) which are the object of vision in our time by agreement: are they an object of touch or not? Some of them held that the perception of touch attaches to them, and argued that whoever touches something and it moves under his hand perceives its motion, and if its parts separate in his hand he perceives their separation. And among the companions is one who denied that, and claimed that he knows that at the time of touch, but the perception of touch does not attach to it.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific disagreement among Ash'ari theologians about the 'akwan' (plural of kawun, meaning the accidents of motion, rest, etc.). These akwan are agreed to be the object of vision (we see motion/rest). The question is whether they are also perceived by touch. Some scholars argue that when we touch a moving object, we directly perceive its motion through touch; similarly, we perceive separation of parts by touch. Others deny that touch perceives the akwan themselves; rather, we infer them from the tactile experience, but touch does not directly attach to them. The passage presents both views without resolution, reflecting that the matter was disputed among the Ash'ari school.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'akwan' means universes or worlds.
Correction: In this kalam context, 'akwan' (plural of kawun) refers to the accidents of motion, rest, etc., not universes. It is a technical term in atomist occasionalism.

Checkpoint
What is the dispute about regarding the 'akwan'?
A) Whether they are visible.
B) Whether they are objects of touch.
C) Whether they are eternal.
D) Whether they are created.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari scholars disagreed on whether the sense of touch directly perceives the accidents of motion and rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that Ash'ari scholars disagreed on whether touch directly perceives motion. When you touch a moving object, do you assume your sense of touch gives you certain knowledge of the motion, or is that knowledge actually a conclusion your mind draws from the sensation? How does this uncertainty about basic sensory perception affect your reliance on your senses when it comes to matters of faith?
?
The second opinion claims that you know the motion 'at the time of touch' but not through touch itself. Think of an area in your life where you have assumed that your direct experience gives you certainty about something beyond the physical. What would change if you admitted that your knowledge might be indirect, and that only revelation can give you true certainty?
?
Imagine you are in a situation where your senses tell you something that contradicts a revealed truth—for example, seeing something that appears to contradict divine wisdom. Would you trust your senses or the revealed teaching? This lesson's technical debate reminds us that even sensory perception is not straightforward. How prepared are you to submit your sensory judgments to divine revelation, especially when they seem obvious?

Objection and Answer on Infinite Regress for Vision of All Existents
Sanusi presents an objection on infinite regress for vision of all existents and the qadi's answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of infinite regress against the Ash'ari claim that vision applies to all existents, and how the qadi answers it.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is stopped by the obstacle's self-prevention.

This passage presents a rigorous dialectical exchange in Islamic theology. The objection challenges the consistency of the Ash'ari doctrine that vision can attach to every existent. By following the logical moves—objection, answer, counter-objection—you will see how kalam thinkers refined their positions through hypothetical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: objection based on infinite regress, then the qadi's answer, then a counter-objection.
2
Identify the objection
Find the core reasoning: vision itself is an existent, so it should be visible. Since we don't see it, there must be an obstacle. That obstacle is also existent, leading to infinite regress.
3
Analyze the answer and counter
The qadi argues the first obstacle prevents its own vision, stopping the regress. Then a counter-objection says this implies the obstacle's invisibility is a self-attribute, which might be problematic. Reflect on whether the answer is satisfactory.
The passage cuts off midsentence; the counter-objection is not fully resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأُورِدَ على أهلِ السنَّةِ في قولهم : ( إِنَّ الرؤية تتعلق بكلِّ موجود ) لزوم التسلسل ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ الرؤية المتعلقة هي مِنْ جملة الموجوداتِ فيلزم أن تصح رؤيتُها ، فإذا لم نرَ رؤيتنا فإنَّما لم نرها لمانع ، كما في حق غيرِها مِنَ الموجوداتِ التي لا نراها ، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى ذلكَ المانع فنقول : هو موجود (٢) ، فيجوز أن يُرى ، فيَحتاجُ أيضاً إلى تقديرِ مانع يمنعُ مِنْ رؤيتِهِ ، وكذا الكلام في مانع المانع . إلى ما لا نهاية له . وأجاب القاضي عن ذلكَ : بأنَّ المانعَ الأَوَّلَ يمنعُ مِنْ رؤية ما هو مانع منه ، ومانع مِنْ رؤية نفسِهِ ، فلا يَحتاجُ إلى تقديرِ مانع آخر حتى يلزم التسلسل . واعتُرِضَ عليهِ : بأنَّ المانعَ إِذا كانَ يمنعُ مِنْ رؤية نفسِهِ فيكونُ امتناع رؤيتِهِ صفةً نفسيَّةً لهُ تمنعُ مِنْ تقديرِ مانع بالنسبة إلى رؤيته .
And an objection was raised against the People of the Sunna concerning their statement: 'Indeed, vision attaches to every existent' — the necessity of infinite regress (tasalsul). That is because the vision that attaches is among the existents, so it necessarily follows that its vision is valid. If we do not see our vision, then we do not see it only because of an obstacle, just as in the case of other existents that we do not see. Then we transfer the discussion to that obstacle and say: it is an existent, so it is permissible to be seen, so it also requires the assumption of an obstacle preventing its vision, and likewise for the obstacle of the obstacle, ad infinitum. And the Qadi answered that: the first obstacle prevents the vision of that which it is an obstacle for, and prevents its own vision, so there is no need to assume another obstacle such that regress follows. And it was objected against him: If the obstacle prevents its own vision, then the impossibility of its vision is a self-attribute of it that prevents the assumption of an obstacle with respect to its vision.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection and response concerning the Ash'ari claim that vision can apply to every existent. The objection argues that this leads to infinite regress: since vision itself is an existent, it should be visible. But we do not see our own vision. So there must be an obstacle preventing it. That obstacle is also an existent, so it too should be visible, requiring another obstacle, and so on infinitely. The qadi (likely al-Baqillani or another Ash'ari authority) answers that the first obstacle prevents both the vision of what it obstructs and its own vision. Therefore, the obstacle's own invisibility is intrinsic; no further obstacle is needed for it. This stops the regress. Then a counter-objection is raised: if the obstacle prevents its own vision, then its non-visibility is a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya) that prevents assuming an obstacle for it. This counter-objection challenges the qadi's solution by pointing out that the obstacle's self-prevention might itself require explanation, but the text cuts off. The passage exemplifies the rigorous logical method of kalam, where a theological claim is tested by hypothetical regresses and responses are refined.

Key Terms
رؤية
موجود
تسلسل
مانع
صفة نفسية
قاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about seeing God (i.e., vision of Allah).
Correction: The objection is about the logical consistency of the claim that vision applies to all existents, not specifically about the vision of God. It is a philosophical exercise in logical consequence.

Checkpoint
How does the qadi answer the objection of infinite regress regarding vision of all existents?
A) He denies that vision is an existent.
B) He says the first obstacle prevents its own vision, so no regress.
C) He accepts the regress but says it is finite.
D) He argues that the obstacle is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The objection of infinite regress forces a refinement: the obstacle's self-prevention stops the regress, but raises further questions about self-attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about matters of faith, do you ever consider that your own reasoning is an 'existent' that you cannot fully see? How does that humbling thought change the way you approach theological debates?
?
The obstacle prevents both its own vision and the vision of what it blocks. Is there an obstacle in your life—pride, distraction, or sin—that prevents you from seeing the truth about yourself and about God? What would it take for you to let God remove that obstacle?
?
The counter-objection suggests that if something prevents its own vision, that is a self-attribute. Do you have self-attributes—deep-seated habits or assumptions—that blind you to certain truths? Will you ask God to reveal those blind spots, even if it is uncomfortable?

The Infinite Regress Objection on Vision and Its Resolution
Sanusi presents the tasalsul objection, al-Qadi's answer, and subsequent critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the infinite regress objection (tasalsul) raised against the Sunni claim that vision applies to every existent, and how al-Qadi and later scholars resolved it without compromising the doctrine.

Core Idea
The infinite regress is broken because the first preventer prevents its own vision.

This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical argument about whether vision itself can be seen. The objection of infinite regress seems to threaten the Sunni position, but al-Qadi offers a clever solution that preserves the universality of vision without falling into an endless chain. Sanusi then evaluates alternative answers and shows why only al-Qadi's is sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening lines where the objection is raised: if vision applies to every existent, then vision itself (as an existent) must be visible. If we do not see our vision, that is due to a preventer, which itself is existent and thus requires another preventer, leading to an infinite regress.
Notice how the regress is constructed step by step.
2
Understand al-Qadi's answer
Al-Qadi responds that the first preventer both prevents the vision of what it prevents and prevents its own vision. Thus, no further preventer is needed, halting the regress.
This is the core insight: the preventer is self-preventing.
3
Examine the objection to al-Qadi
An interlocutor objects that if the preventer's self-prevention is an intrinsic attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya), then the universality of existence as a condition for visibility is undermined.
This challenges the very basis of the original claim.
4
Study al-Qadi's rejoinder
Al-Qadi replies that the prevention is specific to the subject in which it inheres; another subject can see the preventer, so the universality of existence remains valid.
This preserves the original claim while resolving the objection.
5
Reflect on Sanusi's evaluation
Sanusi mentions the alternative answer that Allah cuts the chain by creating sleep, but he refutes it because the regress involves simultaneous infinite preventers, not a sequential chain. Only al-Qadi's answer is sound.
This shows the depth of the dialectic and the importance of precise reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأُورِدَ على أهلِ السنَّةِ في قولهم : ( إِنَّ الرؤية تتعلق بكلِّ موجود ) لزوم التسلسل ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ الرؤية المتعلقة هي مِنْ جملة الموجوداتِ فيلزم أن تصح رؤيتُها ، فإذا لم نرَ رؤيتنا فإنَّما لم نرها لمانع ، كما في حق غيرِها مِنَ الموجوداتِ التي لا نراها ، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى ذلكَ المانع فنقول : هو موجود (۲) ، فيجوز أن يُرى ، فيَحتاجُ أيضاً إلى تقديرِ مانعِ يمنعُ مِنْ رؤيتِهِ ، وكذا الكلام في مانع المانع . إلى ما لا نهاية له وأجاب القاضي عن ذلكَ : بأنَّ المانعَ الأَوَّلَ يمنعُ مِنْ رؤية ما هو مانع منه ، ومانع مِنْ رؤية نفسِهِ ، فلا يَحتاجُ إلى تقديرِ مانع آخر حتى يلزم التسلسل (۳) واعتُرِضَ عليهِ : بأنَّ المانعَ إِذا كانَ يمنعُ مِنْ رؤية نفسِهِ فيكونُ امتناع رؤيتِهِ صفةً نفسيَّةً لهُ تمنعُ مِنْ تقديرِ مانع بالنسبة إلى رؤيته ، وذلكَ ممَّا يقدح في طردِ دلالةِ الوجودِ على صحة تعلقِ الرؤية بكلِّ موجود(۱) فأجاب القاضي : بأنَّ المانعَ مِنْ صفةِ نفسِهِ أَنْ يمنعَ مَنْ قامَ بِهِ رؤيتَهُ ، لا غيرَ مَنْ قامَ بهِ ، فيجوزُ أنْ يراه غيرُ مَنْ قامَ بهِ ؛ إذ الحكم لا يثبتُ في المعنى إلا في محلّ قامَ بهِ ذلك المعنى (٢) ، ولا يناقض ذلك كونُ الوجودِ مُصححاً لرؤية كل موجود . قلتُ : قد اختلف علماؤنا في هذه المسألة على مذاهب (۳) : الأول : مذهبُ الشيخ أنَّ الرؤية يجوز أن تُرى مطلقاً ، وحيث لم تُرَ فلمانع ، وما لزمَ مِنَ التسلسل فجوابه ما سبق عنِ القاضي وأجابَ غيرُهُ : بأنَّ الله تعالى يقطعُ تلك السلسلة يخلق النوم متى شاءَ ؛ بأنْ وهو عندَهُ يُضادُّ الإدراك قلتُ : وهو مردودٌ ; لأنَّ السلسلة التي لزمَتْ إِنَّما هي وجود موانع لا نهاية لها مجتمعةً لا مرتبةً (٤) ، فلم يجي النوم ونحوهُ مِنَ الموتِ والغشية وما في معناها حتى لزم المحالُ ; وهو اجتماع موانع لا نهاية لها في الزمان الفردِ ، وإِنَّما يصح الجواب بالنوم ونحوه لو كانَتِ (۱) حاصله : أن المانع حينئذ موجود ولا يرى، ودليلنا مبني على صحة رؤية كل موجود (۲) المراد بالحكم : الامتناع من الرؤية، كما أن المراد بالمعنى : المانع . (۳) أي : مسألة رؤية الرؤية . (٤) في (ب) : ( مترتبة ) .
And an objection was raised against the People of the Sunnah in their saying: 'Indeed, vision (al-ru'ya) applies to every existent' — the necessity of infinite regress (al-tasalsul); that is because the vision that applies is among existents, so it necessarily follows that its vision is valid. If we do not see our vision, then we do not see it only due to a preventer (man'[?]), as in the case of other existents that we do not see. Then we transfer the discourse to that preventer and say: it is existent, so it may be seen, and it also requires supposing a preventer that prevents its vision, and likewise the discourse about the preventer of the preventer, ad infinitum. And al-Qadi answered that: the first preventer prevents the vision of that which it prevents, and prevents its own vision, so there is no need to suppose another preventer such that infinite regress would follow. And an objection was raised against him: if the preventer prevents its own vision, then the impossibility of seeing it is a self-existent attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) that prevents supposing a preventer with respect to its vision, and that detracts from the universality of the indication of existence (al-wujud) for the validity of vision applying to every existent. So al-Qadi answered: the preventer, by virtue of its self, prevents the vision of the one in whom it inheres, not of other than the one in whom it inheres, so it is permissible that other than the one in whom it inheres sees it; for the judgment does not apply in reality except in the locus in which that reality inheres, and that does not contradict the fact that existence qualifies the vision of every existent. I say: our scholars have differed on this question according to schools: the first is the school of the Shaykh that vision may be seen absolutely, and where it is not seen, it is due to a preventer, and what follows from infinite regress, its answer is what preceded from al-Qadi. And others answered: that Allah, exalted, cuts off that chain by creating sleep whenever He wills; for it, in His view, is contrary to perception. I say: and it is refuted; because the chain that follows is only the existence of preventers with no end, simultaneous, not sequential, so sleep and its like from death, unconsciousness, and what is similar do not come such that the impossible would follow, which is the simultaneity of preventers with no end in a single instant. And the answer by sleep and its like would be valid only if the chain were sequential.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the possibility of seeing the vision itself (ru'ya al-ru'ya). The objection against the Sunni claim that vision applies to every existent is that it leads to an infinite regress: if vision itself is an existent, it too should be visible, and if we do not see it, that is due to a preventer; that preventer is existent and thus should be visible, requiring another preventer, and so on ad infinitum. Al-Qadi (probably al-Baqillani) answers that the first preventer both prevents the vision of what it prevents and prevents its own vision, so no further preventer is needed. An interlocutor objects that if the preventer's self-prevention is an intrinsic attribute, then existence does not universally entail visibility, undermining the original claim. Al-Qadi replies that the prevention is specific to the subject in which it inheres; another subject can see it, so the universality of existence as a condition for visibility remains valid. The author (Sanusi) then notes scholarly disagreements: one opinion (of the Shaykh, presumably Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) holds that vision can be seen absolutely, and its absence is due to a preventer; another opinion invokes sleep as a divinely created interruption that cuts the chain. The author refutes this latter answer, arguing that the required regress is a simultaneous infinite set of preventers, not a temporal sequence, so sleep cannot eliminate an instantaneous infinity. Thus, the only sound answer is al-Qadi's.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
مانع (mani')
وجود (wujud)
صفة نفسية (sifa nafsiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress objection is fatal to the Sunni claim that vision applies to every existent.
Correction: Al-Qadi shows that the regress is avoided because the first preventer prevents its own vision, eliminating the need for an infinite chain.

Checkpoint
What is the core of al-Qadi's answer to the infinite regress objection?
A) The first preventer prevents only the vision of what it prevents, not its own vision.
B) The first preventer prevents its own vision, so no further preventer is needed.
C) The chain is cut by sleep, which Allah creates.
D) The regress is only apparent because the preventer is not an existent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's insight that the first preventer prevents its own vision resolves the infinite regress objection without compromising the universality of vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a debate about whether an infinite chain of preventers can exist. In your own life, what 'preventers' have you used to avoid seeing a painful truth about yourself? Are you willing to let that first preventer be the one that stops the chain—by admitting the truth and repenting?
?
Al-Qadi argued that a preventer only prevents vision for the one in whom it inheres, not for others. Think of a blessing or fault that others see in you but you ignore. Will you ask a trusted friend today what they see that your own 'preventer' (pride, fear, or denial) is hiding from you?
?
The objector thought infinite regress was inevitable, but al-Qadi broke it with a single insight. Is there a sin you have justified by thinking 'if I stop this one, I'll have to stop everything'? Will you now stop that one sin today, trusting that it prevents its own chain?

God's News and Determination: Objection and Answer
Ibn al-Tilimsani answers the objection that God's news requires prior determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Tilimsani resolves the apparent circularity in God's news about determination by distinguishing performative from informative statements.

Core Idea
God's news can create reality, not merely describe it.

This passage presents a sophisticated theological objection: if God's true news must correspond to reality, then announcing a determination implies it already exists, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Ibn al-Tilimsani offers a subtle answer by invoking the concept of performative speech (insha'), which establishes the reality it expresses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage: the objection states that true news must follow the reported thing as it is. Therefore, God's news about a determination would require that determination to exist before the news.
Notice the assumption: all true news describes a pre-existing reality.
2
Recognize the dilemma
The objection then presents two problems: if the determination is caused by the very news, it's circular; if by something else, it leads to infinite regress. Identify why both are problematic.
Circularity means the news would cause itself; regress means an endless chain.
3
Grasp the performative answer
Ibn al-Tilamsini responds by pointing out that some utterances are performative (insha')—they create the reality they express, like 'I divorce.' In such cases, no prior reality is needed. Apply this to God's news about determination.
Think of divine decree as performative: God's announcement itself brings about the determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأُورِدَ عليهِ : أَنَّ خبرَ اللهِ تعالى صدق ، والخبر الصدقُ يتبع المخبر عنه على ما هو بهِ ، فإذا أخبر الله تعالى عن تحتُّمِ شيءٍ .. فلا بد وأن تكون صفة التحتُم ثابتة له قبل الإخبار ، فتحتُمُهُ إنْ كانَ بنفس هذا الخبر دار ، وإِنْ كانَ بغيرِهِ تسلسل . قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْسانيّ : ( ويمكنُ أنْ يجيب : بأنَّ بعض الأخبارِ يُرادُ بها الإنشاء ، فلا يُشترَطُ كونُها بتلك الصفة قبل تعلقِهِ بها ، بل يثبت معها ؛ كقولك : طلقتُ وأعتقتُ ووكلتُ وما أشبه ذلك )
It was objected to him: That the news of God Almighty is true, and true news follows the reported thing as it is. So if God Almighty informs about the determination of something, then the attribute of determination must be established for it before the news. So its determination, if it is by this very news, is circular; and if by something else, it leads to infinite regress. Ibn al-Tilimsani said: 'And it is possible to answer: That some news are intended as performative (insha'), so it is not required that they have that attribute before being attached to it, rather it is established with it; like your saying: I divorced, I emancipated, I delegated, and the like.'
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and an answer. The objector argues that God's true news must correspond to a prior reality, so if God informs of a determination, that determination must already exist. This would lead to a circularity or infinite regress. Ibn al-Tilimsani responds by distinguishing between informative news (khabar) and performative utterances (insha'). Performative statements like 'I divorce' create the reality they express; similarly, God's news about determination can be performative, establishing the determination simultaneously with the announcement.

Key Terms
خبر
صدق
تحتّم
إنشاء
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine declarations are purely informative and must describe a pre-existing reality.
Correction: Some divine declarations are performative: they bring about the reality they express, so no prior determination is required.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that God's news about determination requires prior determination?
A) It is impossible for God to inform without prior determination
B) Some news are performative and create the determination
C) The objection is invalid because God's knowledge is eternal
D) Determination is not needed

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Tilamsini resolves the objection by showing that God's news about determination is performative, not merely informative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated God's promises as merely informative—something to be believed but not expected to actually transform your reality? How would your life change if you truly saw His words as performative, creating what they announce? Commit today to recite one of God's promises and act as if it is already being fulfilled.
?
If God's news about your ultimate fate (like guidance or salvation) can bring that fate into existence, then what does that say about the power of listening to His words with full presence? What holds you back from surrendering to the creative force of His speech? Identify one barrier and bring it to God in prostration.
?
The objection in this lesson assumes God's speech must follow reality, but the answer shows God's speech creates reality. How does your assumption about God's relationship to time affect your trust in His decrees? Write down one area where you find it hard to trust God's decree, and then read this passage again, letting it rewire your assumption.

The Nature of Unity: Negative or Positive Attribute
The author presents the dispute over unity as a negative or positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological dispute over whether unity is a negative or positive attribute and the Ash'ari position that it is negative.

Core Idea
Unity is the negation of multiplicity, not a separate positive attribute.

This short passage records a key disagreement among Islamic theologians: is God's unity (al-wahda) a positive quality added to His essence, or simply the absence of multiplicity? The author presents two views and then gives the verified Ash'ari stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice how the author presents the disagreement, then lists two opposing views, and finally gives his verdict.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'ṣifa salbiyya' and 'ṣifa nafsiyya'.
2
Identify the two views
The first view says unity is a negative attribute, i.e., the negation of multiplicity. The second view, attributed to al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, says it is a positive attribute (nafsiyya).
Proponents of the first view are unnamed; the second view is attributed to specific scholars.
3
Understand the verified position
The author states that the verified position is the first (al-awwal), meaning unity is a negative attribute. This aligns with the Ash'ari theological method that avoids affirming extra positive attributes beyond the divine essence.
The phrase 'wa al-taḥqīq: al-awwal' is a decisive statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف في الوحدة؛ فقيل: هي صفة سلبية، فهي عبارة عن سلب الكثرة، ونقل عن القاضي وإمام الحرمين: أنها صفة نفسية، والتحقيق: الأول.
And there has been disagreement concerning unity (al-wahda); for it was said: It is a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya), so it is an expression of the negation of multiplicity. And it was transmitted from al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn that it is a positive attribute (sifa nafsiyya). And the verified position is the first.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage records a theological disagreement about the nature of the attribute of unity. Some scholars held that unity is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya), meaning it is merely the absence or negation of multiplicity (salb al-kathra). Others, notably al-Qāḍī (likely al-Bāqillānī) and Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), maintained that it is a positive attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya), i.e., a real quality inherent in the thing. The author states that the correct (verifiable) position is the first: unity is a negative attribute. This conclusion aligns with the Ash‘arī view that divine attributes are either negative or relational, and that unity is not an added positive quality but simply the denial of any composition or multiplicity in God.

Key Terms
الوحدة
صفة سلبية
صفة نفسية
سلب الكثرة
القاضي
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think unity is a separate positive attribute of God, adding to His essence.
Correction: The correct Ash'ari view is that unity is a negative attribute, simply denying multiplicity.

Checkpoint

B) It is a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya)
C) It is both negative and positive
D) It is relational

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, unity is understood as the negation of multiplicity, not a positive attribute added to the divine essence.

Can Oneness Be Proven by Scripture Alone?
States the disagreement on proving Oneness by transmitted evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement about whether the doctrine of God's oneness (tawhid) can be established using only transmitted evidence (scripture) without rational proof.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree: can scripture alone prove God's oneness without reason?

This short but important passage from Sanusi highlights a key methodological disagreement in Islamic theology. Some scholars hold that the doctrine of tawhid can be proven directly from revealed texts, while others argue that since the existence of God Himself requires rational proof, so too does His oneness. Let's examine the arguments presented.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the two sides mentioned.
The passage is concise; every word counts.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where the author states that affirming oneness by transmitted evidence is valid, and where some verifiers forbid it.
3
Understand the reasoning of the verifiers
Focus on the sentence starting with 'because the affirmation of the Maker...'. Understand why they think transmitted evidence cannot establish oneness.
The key point: the Maker's existence itself cannot be known through scripture alone.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Ask yourself why this disagreement matters. How does it relate to the role of reason and revelation in theology?
This touches on a foundational issue in kalam: the relationship between reason and transmitted knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في صحة الاستدلال على ثبوتِ الوحدانية بالدليل السمعي وحده. ويصح إثباتُ هذا العَقْدِ ـ وهو الوحدانية - بالدليل السمعي ، ومنعَهُ بعضُ المحققين ، وهو رأي ؛ لأنَّ ثبوت الصانع لا يتحقق بدونِها ، ولا أثر للدليل السمعي في ثبوتِ الصانع، فكذا ما يتوقَّفُ عليه ، والله أعلم .
The disagreement concerning the validity of inference to the affirmation of Oneness (al-wahdaniyya) by transmitted evidence alone. And it is valid to affirm this creed — which is Oneness — by transmitted evidence, and some of the verifiers forbade it, and it is an opinion; because the affirmation of the Maker (al-Ṣāniʿ) is not realized without it, and there is no effect of transmitted evidence in affirming the Maker, and likewise what depends on it, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage introduces a disagreement among scholars about whether the doctrine of God's oneness (al-wahdaniyya) can be proven using only transmitted evidence (al-dalīl al-samʿī), i.e., scripture, without rational proof. The author states that it is valid to affirm this creed by transmitted evidence alone, but some verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) forbid it. Their reasoning is that the affirmation of the Maker (al-Ṣāniʿ) itself cannot be established without rational proof, and transmitted evidence has no role in that, so likewise what depends on it (i.e., oneness) cannot be established by transmitted evidence alone. The author ends with 'and God knows best', leaving the matter open.
